Road transport of the weapon was hazardous. As a safety measure 120,000 steel ball bearings were used to fill a cavity inside the core and keep the fissile components apart. In an accident, the steel bung was removed and the ball bearings spilled on the floor of an aircraft hangar, leaving the bomb armed and dangerous.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2010103\xe2\x80\x93105_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2010103\xe2\x80\x93105-127">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-info_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-info-128">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> About ten were delivered.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynn1997242\xe2\x80\x93243_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynn1997242\xe2\x80\x93243-129">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Vickers_Valiant_(27363801724).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Vickers_Valiant_%2827363801724%29.jpg/220px-Vickers_Valiant_%2827363801724%29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Vickers_Valiant_%2827363801724%29.jpg/330px-Vickers_Valiant_%2827363801724%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Vickers_Valiant_%2827363801724%29.jpg/440px-Vickers_Valiant_%2827363801724%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5472" data-file-height="3648" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Vickers_Valiant_(27363801724).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Vickers Valiant XD818 at the <a href="/wiki/RAF_Museum_Cosford" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF Museum Cosford">RAF Museum Cosford</a>  was the aircraft that dropped the bomb in the Grapple 1 test in May 1957.</div></div></div>\n<p>The scientists at Aldermaston had not yet mastered the design of thermonuclear weapons. They produced a new design, called Round A.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001149\xe2\x80\x93153_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001149\xe2\x80\x93153-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcIntyre200634\xe2\x80\x9336_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcIntyre200634\xe2\x80\x9336-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> Another trial was scheduled, known as Grapple X.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001149\xe2\x80\x93153_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001149\xe2\x80\x93153-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHubbardSimmons2008157_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHubbardSimmons2008157-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> Round A was dropped on 8 November 1957.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHubbardSimmons2008162_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHubbardSimmons2008162-133">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001160\xe2\x80\x93161_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001160\xe2\x80\x93161-134">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> To save time and money,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001149\xe2\x80\x93153_130-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001149\xe2\x80\x93153-130">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> the target was off the southern tip of Christmas Island rather than off Malden Island, just 20 nautical miles (37&#160;km; 23&#160;mi) from the airfield where 3,000 men were based.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHubbardSimmons2008157_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHubbardSimmons2008157-132">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> This time the yield of 1.8 megatonnes of TNT (7.5&#160;PJ) exceeded expectations. Round A was a true hydrogen bomb, but it used a relatively large quantity of expensive highly enriched uranium.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001160\xe2\x80\x93162_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001160\xe2\x80\x93162-135">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Aldermaston had plenty of ideas about how to follow up Grapple X. A new design used <a href="/wiki/Lithium_deuteride" class="mw-redirect" title="Lithium deuteride">lithium deuteride</a> that was less enriched in <a href="/wiki/Lithium-6" class="mw-redirect" title="Lithium-6">lithium-6</a> (and therefore had more <a href="/wiki/Lithium-7" class="mw-redirect" title="Lithium-7">lithium-7</a>), but more of it, thereby reducing the amount of uranium-235 in the core. Because of the possibility of an international moratorium on atmospheric testing, plans for the trial, codenamed Grapple Y, were given verbal approval by the Prime Minister, and known only to a handful of officials.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001165\xe2\x80\x93167_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001165\xe2\x80\x93167-136">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> The bomb was dropped off Christmas on 28 April 1958.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHubbardSimmons2008170_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHubbardSimmons2008170-137">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001173\xe2\x80\x93174_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001173\xe2\x80\x93174-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> It had an explosive yield of about 3 megatonnes of TNT (13&#160;PJ), and remains the largest British nuclear weapon ever tested.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001173\xe2\x80\x93174_138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001173\xe2\x80\x93174-138">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> The design of Grapple Y was notably successful because much of its yield came from its thermonuclear reaction instead of fission of a uranium-238 tamper, making it a true hydrogen bomb, and because its yield had been correctly predicted\xe2\x80\x94indicating that its designers understood what they were doing.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcIntyre200634\xe2\x80\x9336_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcIntyre200634\xe2\x80\x9336-131">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaylis1994170_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaylis1994170-139">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>On 22 August 1958, US President <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> announced a moratorium on nuclear testing. This did not mean an immediate end to testing; on the contrary, the United States, the Soviet Union and the United Kingdom all rushed to perform as much testing as possible before the deadline.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHewlettHoll1989547\xe2\x80\x93548_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHewlettHoll1989547\xe2\x80\x93548-140">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> A new British test series, known as Grapple Z, commenced on 22 August. It explored new technologies such as the use of external neutron initiators, which had first been tried out with Orange Herald. Core boosting using <a href="/wiki/Tritium" title="Tritium">tritium</a> and external boosting with layers of lithium deuteride were successfully tested, allowing a smaller, lighter two-stage devices.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001177\xe2\x80\x93182_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001177\xe2\x80\x93182-141">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> The international moratorium commenced on 31 October 1958, and Britain ceased atmospheric testing for good.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001191_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001191-142">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="An_independent_deterrent">An independent deterrent</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: An independent deterrent">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Believing that the United Kingdom was extremely vulnerable to a nuclear attack to which defence was impossible, the Chiefs of Staff and the RAF first advocated a British nuclear deterrence\xe2\x80\x94not just nuclear weapons\xe2\x80\x94in 1945: "It is our opinion that our only chance of securing a quick decision is by launching a devastating attack upon [enemy cities] with absolute weapons."  In 1947 the Chiefs of Staff stated that even with American help the United Kingdom could not prevent the "vastly superior" Soviet forces from overrunning Western Europe, from which Russia could destroy Britain with missiles without using atomic weapons. Only "the threat of large-scale damage from similar weapons" could prevent the Soviet Union from using atomic weapons in a war.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaylis199548,_397\xe2\x80\x93398_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaylis199548,_397\xe2\x80\x93398-143">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Air_Chief_Marshal" class="mw-redirect" title="Air Chief Marshal">Air Chief Marshal</a> Sir <a href="/wiki/John_Slessor" title="John Slessor">John Slessor</a>, who became Chief of the Air Staff in 1950, wrote that year that the Soviet superiority in European forces was so great that even "an ultimatum by Russia within the next two to three years" might cause Western Europe to surrender without a war. He feared that the United Kingdom might also do so "unless we can make ourselves far less defenceless than we are now." By 1952 the <a href="/wiki/Air_Ministry" title="Air Ministry">Air Ministry</a> had abandoned the concept of a conventional defence of Western Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBall199571,_78\xe2\x80\x9379_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBall199571,_78\xe2\x80\x9379-144">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup> The hydrogen bomb increased the threat to Britain. In 1957, a government study stated that although RAF fighters would "unquestionably be able to take a heavy toll of enemy bombers, a proportion would inevitably get through. Even if it were only a dozen, they could with megaton bombs inflict widespread devastation." Although disarmament remained a British goal, "the only existing safeguard against major aggression is the power to threaten retaliation with nuclear weapons."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaylis1995429\xe2\x80\x93430_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaylis1995429\xe2\x80\x93430-145">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Churchill stated in a 1955 speech that deterrence would be "the parents of disarmament" and that, unless Britain contributed to Western deterrence with its own weapons, during a war the targets that threatened it the most might not be prioritised. The Prime Minister, <a href="/wiki/Harold_Macmillan" title="Harold Macmillan">Harold Macmillan</a>, advanced the position that nuclear weapons would give Britain influence over targeting and American policy, and would affect strategy in the Middle East and Far East. His <a href="/wiki/Secretary_of_State_for_Defence" title="Secretary of State for Defence">Minister of Defence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duncan_Sandys" title="Duncan Sandys">Duncan Sandys</a>, considered that nuclear weapons reduced Britain\'s dependence on the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGott1963248_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGott1963248-146">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> The 1956 <a href="/wiki/Suez_Crisis" title="Suez Crisis">Suez Crisis</a> demonstrated that Britain was longer a great power,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBowiePlatt198443_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowiePlatt198443-147">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> but increased the value to Britain of an independent nuclear deterrent that would give it greater influence with the US and USSR.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEpstein1966141_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEpstein1966141-148">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> While the military target of British nuclear weapons was the Soviet Union, the political target was the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore201028_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore201028-149">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:100_years_of_the_RAF_MOD_45163623.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/100_years_of_the_RAF_MOD_45163623.jpg/220px-100_years_of_the_RAF_MOD_45163623.jpg" width="220" height="277" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/100_years_of_the_RAF_MOD_45163623.jpg/330px-100_years_of_the_RAF_MOD_45163623.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/100_years_of_the_RAF_MOD_45163623.jpg/440px-100_years_of_the_RAF_MOD_45163623.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4365" data-file-height="5500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:100_years_of_the_RAF_MOD_45163623.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Avro_Vulcan" title="Avro Vulcan">Avro Vulcan</a> bombers</div></div></div>\n<p>Independent targeting was vital. The Chiefs of Staff believed that\xe2\x80\x94contrary to Tizard\'s view\xe2\x80\x94once the Soviet Union became able to attack the United States itself with nuclear weapons in the late 1950s, America might not risk its own cities to defend Europe, or not emphasise targets that endangered the United Kingdom more than the United States:<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaylis1995185\xe2\x80\x93187_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaylis1995185\xe2\x80\x93187-150">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWheeler1985\xe2\x80\x93198681\xe2\x80\x9384_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWheeler1985\xe2\x80\x93198681\xe2\x80\x9384-151">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>When New York is vulnerable to attack the United States will not use her strategic weapon in defence of London. The United Kingdom must, therefore, have its own retaliatory defence. Similarly, however, we will not be prepared to sacrifice the United Kingdom in the defence of say <a href="/wiki/Darwin,_Australia" class="mw-redirect" title="Darwin, Australia">Darwin</a>, and eventually each political unit must have its own means of retaliation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaylis1995416_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaylis1995416-152">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWheeler1985\xe2\x80\x93198683_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWheeler1985\xe2\x80\x93198683-153">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> \n</p></blockquote><p>\nBritain thus needed the ability to convince the USSR that attacking Europe would be too costly regardless of American participation. Part of the perceived effectiveness of an independent deterrent was the willingness to target enemy cities. Slessor saw atomic weapons as a way to avoid a third devastating world war given that the two previous ones had begun without them. While he sought to de-emphasise city targeting in British plans as Air Chief,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaylis1995110\xe2\x80\x93114_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaylis1995110\xe2\x80\x93114-154">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Slessor wrote in 1954 after retirement:</p><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>And if [war] is forced upon us, we must be able to instantly deliver a crushing counter-attack upon aggression at its source\xe2\x80\x94not merely at its airfields, its launching sites and submarine bases, at its armies in the field but at the heart of the aggressor country. There will be the battlefield if battlefield there must be.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWheeler1985\xe2\x80\x93198682_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWheeler1985\xe2\x80\x93198682-155">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> \n</p></blockquote><p>\nWhen <a href="/wiki/Air_Marshal" class="mw-redirect" title="Air Marshal">Air Marshal</a> Sir <a href="/wiki/George_Mills_(RAF_officer)" title="George Mills (RAF officer)">George Mills</a> became head of RAF Bomber Command in 1955 he similarly insisted on targeting Soviet cities, writing "Whoever would be afraid of launching a sudden attack if he thought the greater part of our retaliation would come back to his airfields?"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung200711_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung200711-156">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> The belief in the importance of retaining an independent capability has continued over several decades and changes in government. As the Defence Council stated in 1980:</p><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>our force has to be visibly capable of making a massive strike on its own ... We need to convince Soviet leaders that even if they thought ... the US would hold back, the British force could still inflict a blow so destructive that the penalty for aggression would have proved too high.<sup id="cite_ref-Future_Deterrent_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Future_Deterrent-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>When France developed its own nuclear weapons, British politicians contended that Europe required an independent deterrent other than that of France.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoddart2014b195_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoddart2014b195-158">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Renewed_American_partnership">Renewed American partnership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Renewed American partnership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/1958_US-UK_Mutual_Defence_Agreement" class="mw-redirect" title="1958 US-UK Mutual Defence Agreement">1958 US-UK Mutual Defence Agreement</a> and <a href="/wiki/Special_relationship" class="mw-redirect" title="Special relationship">Special relationship</a></div>\n<p>The Soviet Union\'s launch of <a href="/wiki/Sputnik_1" title="Sputnik 1">Sputnik 1</a>, the world\'s first <a href="/wiki/Artificial_satellite" class="mw-redirect" title="Artificial satellite">artificial satellite</a>, on 4 October 1957, came as a tremendous shock to the American public, who had trusted that American technological superiority ensured their invulnerability. Now, suddenly, there was incontrovertible proof that, in some areas at least, the Soviet Union was actually ahead. In the widespread calls for action in response to the <a href="/wiki/Sputnik_crisis" title="Sputnik crisis">Sputnik crisis</a>, officials in the United States and Britain seized an opportunity to mend the relationship with Britain that had been damaged by the Suez Crisis.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBotti1987199\xe2\x80\x93201_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBotti1987199\xe2\x80\x93201-159">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> Macmillan wrote to Eisenhower on 10 October urging that the two countries pool their resources to meet the challenge.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001199_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001199-160">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>British information security, or the lack thereof, no longer seemed so important now that the Soviet Union was apparently ahead, and British scientists had demonstrated that they understood how to build a hydrogen bomb with a different form of the <a href="/wiki/Teller-Ulam" class="mw-redirect" title="Teller-Ulam">Teller-Ulam</a> design to the Americans. The opposition that had derailed previous attempts was now absent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBotti1987224\xe2\x80\x93225_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBotti1987224\xe2\x80\x93225-161">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> The McMahon Act was amended,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBotti1987234\xe2\x80\x93236_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBotti1987234\xe2\x80\x93236-162">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> paving the way for the <a href="/wiki/1958_US%E2%80%93UK_Mutual_Defence_Agreement" title="1958 US\xe2\x80\x93UK Mutual Defence Agreement">1958 US\xe2\x80\x93UK Mutual Defence Agreement</a> (MDA).<sup id="cite_ref-full_text_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-full_text-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBotti1987238_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBotti1987238-164">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> Macmillan called this "the Great Prize".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacmillan1971323_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacmillan1971323-165">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Under the MDA, 5.37 tonnes of UK-produced plutonium was sent to the US in exchange for 6.7&#160;kg  of tritium and 7.5 tonnes of HEU between 1960 and 1979. A further 470&#160;kg  of plutonium was swapped between the US and the UK for reasons that remain classified.<sup id="cite_ref-mod-plutonium-account_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mod-plutonium-account-166">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup>  Much of the HEU supplied by the US was used not for weapons, but as fuel for the growing fleet of UK <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_submarine" title="Nuclear submarine">nuclear submarines</a>. Under the MDA, the US supplied the UK with not just nuclear submarine propulsion technology, but a complete <a href="/wiki/S5W" class="mw-redirect" title="S5W">S5W</a> <a href="/wiki/Pressurised_water_reactor" class="mw-redirect" title="Pressurised water reactor">pressurised water reactor</a> of the kind used to power the US <a href="/wiki/Skipjack-class_submarine" title="Skipjack-class submarine"><i>Skipjack</i>-class</a> submarines. This was used in the Royal Navy\'s first nuclear-powered submarine, <a href="/wiki/HMS_Dreadnought_(S101)" title="HMS Dreadnought (S101)">HMS&#160;<i>Dreadnought</i></a>, which was <a href="/wiki/Ceremonial_ship_launching" title="Ceremonial ship launching">launched</a> in 1960 and <a href="/wiki/Ship_commissioning" title="Ship commissioning">commissioned</a> in 1963. The S5W had a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor_core" title="Nuclear reactor core">Nuclear reactor core</a> that used uranium enriched to between 93 and 97 per cent uranium-235.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERitchie20153_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERitchie20153-167">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> Reactor technology was transferred from Westinghouse to <a href="/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Limited" title="Rolls-Royce Limited">Rolls Royce</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore201035_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore201035-168">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> which used it as the basis for its <a href="/wiki/PWR1" class="mw-redirect" title="PWR1">PWR1</a> reactor used in the UK\'s nuclear  submarines.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERitchie20154_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERitchie20154-169">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The MDA has been renewed or amended many times. Most amendments merely extended the treaty for another five or ten years; others added definitions and made minor changes.<sup id="cite_ref-full_text_163-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-full_text-163">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> As of  2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the most recent renewal was on 22 July 2014, extending the treaty to 31 December 2024.<sup id="cite_ref-2014_amendment_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2014_amendment-172">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> A 1974 US proliferation report discussing British nuclear and missile development noted that "In many cases, it is based on technology received from the US and could not legitimately be passed on without US permission."<sup id="cite_ref-SNIE-4-1-74_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SNIE-4-1-74-174">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="US_nuclear_weapons">US nuclear weapons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: US nuclear weapons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Project_E" title="Project E">Project E</a></div>\n<p>Production of British nuclear weapons was slow and Britain had only ten atomic bombs on hand in 1955 and just fourteen in 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung2016201_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung2016201-175">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> At the three-power Bermuda Conference with Eisenhower in December 1953, Churchill, suggested that the United States allow Britain to have access to American nuclear weapons to make up the shortfall.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaul2000200\xe2\x80\x93201_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaul2000200\xe2\x80\x93201-176">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> The provision on American weapons was called <a href="/wiki/Project_E" title="Project E">Project E</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynn1997254\xe2\x80\x93256_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynn1997254\xe2\x80\x93256-177">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup> The agreement was confirmed by Eisenhower and Macmillan, who was now the Prime Minister, during their March 1957 meeting in Bermuda,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynn1997258_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynn1997258-178">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaylis1995258_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaylis1995258-179">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> and a formal <a href="/wiki/Memorandum_of_Understanding" class="mw-redirect" title="Memorandum of Understanding">Memorandum of Understanding</a> (MOU) was signed on 21 May 1957.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynn1997259_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynn1997259-180">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2010114_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2010114-181">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:English_Electric_Canberra_TT18,_UK_-_Air_Force_AN2276146.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/English_Electric_Canberra_TT18%2C_UK_-_Air_Force_AN2276146.jpg/220px-English_Electric_Canberra_TT18%2C_UK_-_Air_Force_AN2276146.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/English_Electric_Canberra_TT18%2C_UK_-_Air_Force_AN2276146.jpg/330px-English_Electric_Canberra_TT18%2C_UK_-_Air_Force_AN2276146.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/English_Electric_Canberra_TT18%2C_UK_-_Air_Force_AN2276146.jpg/440px-English_Electric_Canberra_TT18%2C_UK_-_Air_Force_AN2276146.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="683" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:English_Electric_Canberra_TT18,_UK_-_Air_Force_AN2276146.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/English_Electric_Canberra" title="English Electric Canberra">English Electric Canberra</a> bomber</div></div></div>\n<p>Four squadrons of <a href="/wiki/English_Electric_Canberra" title="English Electric Canberra">English Electric Canberra</a> bombers based in Germany were equipped with US <a href="/wiki/Mark_7_nuclear_bomb" title="Mark 7 nuclear bomb">Mark 7 nuclear bombs</a> stored at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF Germany">RAF Germany</a> bases. There were also four squadrons of nuclear-armed Canberras based in the UK, which were capable of carrying either the Mark 7 or Red Beard. They too were assigned to the <a href="/wiki/SACEUR" class="mw-redirect" title="SACEUR">SACEUR</a> in October 1960.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore201098\xe2\x80\x9399_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore201098\xe2\x80\x9399-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> The planned V-bomber force was reduced to 144 aircraft, and it was intended to equip half of them with Project E weapons,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronk2014980_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronk2014980-183">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> so 72 <a href="/wiki/Mark_5_nuclear_bomb" title="Mark 5 nuclear bomb">Mark 5 nuclear bombs</a> were supplied for the V-bombers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynn1997264\xe2\x80\x93265_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynn1997264\xe2\x80\x93265-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2010114,_256_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2010114,_256-185">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup> When the MDA came into force, the US agreed to supply the V-bombers with megaton weapons in place of the Mark 5,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung2016212_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung2016212-186">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> in the form of <a href="/wiki/Mark_15_nuclear_bomb" title="Mark 15 nuclear bomb">Mark 15</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mark_39_nuclear_bomb" title="Mark 39 nuclear bomb">Mark 39 nuclear bombs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynn1997264\xe2\x80\x93265_184-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynn1997264\xe2\x80\x93265-184">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Under the Project E MOU, US personnel had custody of the weapons. This meant they performed all the tasks related to their storage, maintenance and readiness. The bombs were stored in Secure Storage Areas (SSAs) on the same bases as the bombers which British staff were not permitted to enter. It was therefore impossible to store British and Americans bombs together in the same SSA.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronk2014978\xe2\x80\x93980_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronk2014978\xe2\x80\x93980-187">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> US custody also created operational problems. The procedure for handing over the bombs added an extra ten minutes to the bombers\' reaction time,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBronk2014985_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBronk2014985-188">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> and the requirement that US personnel had guardianship of the weapons at all times meant that neither they nor the bombers could be relocated to dispersal airfields as the RAF desired.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynn1997262\xe2\x80\x93263_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynn1997262\xe2\x80\x93263-189">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> The operational restrictions imposed by Project E "effectively handed the US government a veto over the use of half of Britain\'s nuclear deterrent".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung2007994_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung2007994-190">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Air Council decided on 7 July 1960 that Project E weapons would be phased out by December 1962, by which time it was anticipated that there would be sufficient British megaton weapons to equip the entire <a href="/wiki/Strategic_bomber" title="Strategic bomber">strategic bomber</a> force.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynn1997266\xe2\x80\x93267_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynn1997266\xe2\x80\x93267-191">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup> Project E weapons were replaced by British <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Sun_(nuclear_weapon)" title="Yellow Sun (nuclear weapon)">Yellow Sun</a> bombs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2010114,_210_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2010114,_210-192">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> Problems encountered in the development <a href="/wiki/Red_Beard" title="Red Beard">Red Beard</a> bomb meant that the replacement of kiloton weapons took longer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung2016213\xe2\x80\x93214_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung2016213\xe2\x80\x93214-193">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> The Air Ministry decided to replace the Canberras with Valiants as the long-range Vulcan and Victor V bombers became available. A Valiant squadron at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Marham" title="RAF Marham">RAF Marham</a> was assigned to SACEUR on 1 January 1961, followed by two more in July. The UK-based Canberra squadrons were then disbanded. Each of the 24 Valiants was equipped with two Project E <a href="/wiki/B28_nuclear_bomb" title="B28 nuclear bomb">Mark 28 nuclear bombs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore201098\xe2\x80\x9399_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore201098\xe2\x80\x9399-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> These were replaced by the newer <a href="/wiki/B43_nuclear_bomb" title="B43 nuclear bomb">Mark 43 nuclear bombs</a> in early 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore201098\xe2\x80\x9399_182-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore201098\xe2\x80\x9399-182">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup> The Valiants were withdrawn from service in 1965.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynn1997371\xe2\x80\x93372_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynn1997371\xe2\x80\x93372-194">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Project E nuclear warheads were also used on the sixty <a href="/wiki/PGM-17_Thor" title="PGM-17 Thor">Thor</a> <a href="/wiki/Intermediate_Range_Ballistic_Missile" class="mw-redirect" title="Intermediate Range Ballistic Missile">Intermediate Range Ballistic Missiles</a> (IRBMs) that were operated by the RAF from 1959 to 1963 under <a href="/wiki/Project_Emily" title="Project Emily">Project Emily</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung201698\xe2\x80\x9399_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung201698\xe2\x80\x9399-195">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Missile_Crisis" title="Cuban Missile Crisis">Cuban Missile Crisis</a>, the RAF\'s bombers and Thor missiles targeted 16 cities, 44 airfields, 10 air defence control centres and 20 IRBM sites.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaylisStoddart2015221_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaylisStoddart2015221-196">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup> The RAF high command never warmed to missiles, and always ranked them secondary to the V bomber force. The missile bases were separate from the rest of the RAF and its personnel considered outside the mainstream. Project Emily gave the RAF considerable experience in missile operations, but the 1960 cancellation Blue Streak in favour of the American <a href="/wiki/GAM-87_Skybolt" title="GAM-87 Skybolt">Skybolt</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Air-launched_ballistic_missile" title="Air-launched ballistic missile">air-launched ballistic missile</a>, rendered this expertise of dubious value.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore201048,_99\xe2\x80\x93100_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore201048,_99\xe2\x80\x93100-197">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> An Air Council meeting on 31 May 1962 decided that Project Emily should be terminated by the end of 1963,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynn1997358\xe2\x80\x93360_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynn1997358\xe2\x80\x93360-198">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> and the last Thor squadrons were inactivated on 23 August 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynn1997362_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynn1997362-199">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RAF_Museum_Cosford_-_DSC08475.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/RAF_Museum_Cosford_-_DSC08475.JPG/220px-RAF_Museum_Cosford_-_DSC08475.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/RAF_Museum_Cosford_-_DSC08475.JPG/330px-RAF_Museum_Cosford_-_DSC08475.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/RAF_Museum_Cosford_-_DSC08475.JPG/440px-RAF_Museum_Cosford_-_DSC08475.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RAF_Museum_Cosford_-_DSC08475.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Skybolt missile</div></div></div>\n<p>The availability of US weapons and designs under the MDA led to the cancellation of several research projects. <a href="/w/index.php?title=Indigo_Hammer&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Indigo Hammer (page does not exist)">Indigo Hammer</a> and the smaller Pixie were warheads intended for use with the <a href="/wiki/Red_Duster_(missile)" class="mw-redirect" title="Red Duster (missile)">Red Duster</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seaslug_(missile)" title="Seaslug (missile)">Seaslug</a> <a href="/wiki/Surface-to-air_missile" title="Surface-to-air missile">surface-to-air missiles</a>; a British version of the US <a href="/wiki/W44" class="mw-redirect" title="W44">W44</a> was chosen instead.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2010126_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2010126-200">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Blue_Peacock" title="Blue Peacock">Blue Peacock</a>, a 16,000-pound (7,300&#160;kg) <a href="/wiki/Atomic_demolition_munition" title="Atomic demolition munition">atomic demolition munition</a> (ADMs) based on Blue Danube, was cancelled in 1958 in favour of the lighter <a href="/w/index.php?title=Violet_Mist&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Violet Mist (page does not exist)">Violet Mist</a>, based on Red Beard. The development of the even smaller and lighter US ADMs led to its cancellation as well in 1961. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHawkings200242\xe2\x80\x9343_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHawkings200242\xe2\x80\x9343-201">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2010132\xe2\x80\x93133_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2010132\xe2\x80\x93133-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> The US <a href="/wiki/Medium_Atomic_Demolition_Munition" title="Medium Atomic Demolition Munition">Medium Atomic Demolition Munition</a> with the <a href="/wiki/W45" title="W45">W45</a> was acquired instead.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorrisKristensen201373_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorrisKristensen201373-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/w/index.php?title=Yellow_Anvil&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Yellow Anvil (page does not exist)">Yellow Anvil</a> was a British artillery warhead. It was cancelled in 1958, and the US supplied the <a href="/wiki/British_Army_of_the_Rhine" title="British Army of the Rhine">British Army of the Rhine</a> (BAOR) with 36 <a href="/wiki/W33_(nuclear_warhead)" title="W33 (nuclear warhead)">W33 nuclear warheads</a> that equipped four batteries of eight-inch <a href="/wiki/M115_howitzer" title="M115 howitzer">M115 howitzers</a>. These were later replaced by <a href="/wiki/M110_howitzer" title="M110 howitzer">M110 howitzers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoddart2012109,_313_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoddart2012109,_313-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2010132\xe2\x80\x93133_202-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2010132\xe2\x80\x93133-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The British Army purchased 113 <a href="/wiki/MGM-5_Corporal" title="MGM-5 Corporal">Corporal missiles</a> from the United States in 1954. It was intended that they would be equipped with British warheads under a project codenamed <a href="/w/index.php?title=Violet_Vision&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Violet Vision (page does not exist)">Violet Vision</a>, but Project E offered a quicker, simpler and cheaper alternative.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMacDonald200659\xe2\x80\x9360_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMacDonald200659\xe2\x80\x9360-205">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> The US supplied 100 W7 warheads, which had to be drawn from US Army storage sites in southern Germany until arrangements were made for local storage in August 1959.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2010130_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2010130-206">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoddart2012109,_313_204-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoddart2012109,_313-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> A British missile, <a href="/wiki/Blue_Water_(missile)" title="Blue Water (missile)">Blue Water</a>, with an Indigo Hammer warhead, was developed to replace Corporal. The US offered the <a href="/wiki/Honest_John_missile" class="mw-redirect" title="Honest John missile">Honest John missile</a> as an interim replacement. The offer was accepted, and 120 Honest John missiles with <a href="/wiki/W31" title="W31">W31</a> warheads were supplied in 1960, enough to equip three artillery regiments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoddart2012109,_313_204-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoddart2012109,_313-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2010132\xe2\x80\x93133_202-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2010132\xe2\x80\x93133-202">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Blue Water was cancelled in July 1962,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2010162_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2010162-207">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> and Honest John remained in service until 1977, when it was replaced by the <a href="/wiki/MGM-52_Lance" title="MGM-52 Lance">Lance missile</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoddart2012109,_313_204-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoddart2012109,_313-204">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup> The British Army deployed more US nuclear weapons than the RAF and Royal Navy combined, peaking at 327 out of 392 in 1976\xe2\x80\x931978.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENorrisKristensen201373_203-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENorrisKristensen201373-203">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A maritime version of Project E, known as Project N supplied US Navy weapons. Providing American atomic bombs for Royal Navy ships would have involved similar dual key arrangements and detachments of <a href="/wiki/US_Marines" class="mw-redirect" title="US Marines">US Marines</a> on board Royal Navy ships, which was deemed impractical even for ships and weapons dedicated to use in European waters. However, RAF <a href="/wiki/Coastal_Command" class="mw-redirect" title="Coastal Command">Coastal Command</a>  acquired <a href="/wiki/Mk_101_Lulu" title="Mk 101 Lulu">Mk 101 Lulu</a> <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_depth_bomb" title="Nuclear depth bomb">nuclear depth bombs</a> (with the <a href="/wiki/W34_(nuclear_warhead)" title="W34 (nuclear warhead)">W34 nuclear warhead</a>) for its <a href="/wiki/Avro_Shackleton" title="Avro Shackleton">Avro Shackleton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hawker_Siddeley_Nimrod" title="Hawker Siddeley Nimrod">Hawker Siddeley Nimrod</a> maritime patrol aircraft from 1965 to 1971 under Project N.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2010221\xe2\x80\x93222_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2010221\xe2\x80\x93222-208">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoddart2012220,_314_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoddart2012220,_314-209">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup> These were later replaced by the more capable <a href="/wiki/B57_nuclear_bomb" title="B57 nuclear bomb">Mark 57</a>, which was stockpiled at <a href="/wiki/RAF_St_Mawgan" title="RAF St Mawgan">RAF St Mawgan</a> and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Machrihanish" title="RAF Machrihanish">RAF Machrihanish</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>When the Cold War ended in 1991, the BAOR still had about 85 Lance missiles, and more than 70 W33 eight-inch and W48 155&#160;mm nuclear artillery shells. The last Project E warheads, including the Mark 57 nuclear depth bombs and those used by the BAOR, were withdrawn in July 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERitchie201414\xe2\x80\x9316_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERitchie201414\xe2\x80\x9316-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="British_nuclear_weapons">British nuclear weapons</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: British nuclear weapons">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Yellow_Sun_(nuclear_weapon)" title="Yellow Sun (nuclear weapon)">Yellow Sun (nuclear weapon)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_Beard" title="Red Beard">Red Beard</a>, and <a href="/wiki/WE.177" title="WE.177">WE.177</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:YellowSunBomb1.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/YellowSunBomb1.JPG/220px-YellowSunBomb1.JPG" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/YellowSunBomb1.JPG/330px-YellowSunBomb1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/YellowSunBomb1.JPG/440px-YellowSunBomb1.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="1584" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:YellowSunBomb1.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Yellow_Sun_(nuclear_weapon)" title="Yellow Sun (nuclear weapon)">Yellow Sun</a>, Britain\'s first production thermonuclear bomb</div></div></div>\n<p>The MDA made fully developed and tested American designs available quickly and cheaply. The first of these was the Mark 28, which was "Anglicised" and manufactured in the UK as <a href="/wiki/Red_Snow" title="Red Snow">Red Snow</a>. Exact copies of American designs were not pursued; the high explosive used in American warheads were more sensitive that British high explosive, and had caused fatal accidents in the US. Its use was not contemplated in the UK after an accident at Aldermaston on 28 February 1959 when two men were killed after a piece of British high explosive fell from a lorry. British high explosive was also bulkier, so a redesign was required.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore201089\xe2\x80\x9390_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore201089\xe2\x80\x9390-212">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Red Snow was far more economical in its use of fissile material than the Green Grass warhead in the Yellow Sun Mk.1 bomb, Britain\'s first production hydrogen bomb. A Yellow Sun Mk.2 with Red Snow, therefore, cost \xc2\xa3500,000 compared to \xc2\xa31.2 million for the Mk.1. RAF Bomber Command wanted Violet Club replaced as soon as possible, so 37 Yellow Sun Mk.1s were delivered by the end of 1959. Deliveries of the Yellow Sun Mk.2 commenced in January 1961, and 43 were delivered by the end of the year. In November 1958. Red Snow also replaced Green Grass as the warhead in the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Steel_missile" class="mw-redirect" title="Blue Steel missile">Blue Steel</a> stand-off missile.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2010104\xe2\x80\x93106_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2010104\xe2\x80\x93106-213">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The kiloton Red Beard, was developed for use by the Canberras and the Royal Navy\'s <a href="/wiki/Fleet_Air_Arm" title="Fleet Air Arm">Fleet Air Arm</a>. Technical problems delayed its introduction into service,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2010113\xe2\x80\x93116_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2010113\xe2\x80\x93116-214">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup> but over 100 were delivered by the end of 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2010217_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2010217-215">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> Up to 48 Red Beards were secretly stowed in a highly secured weapons storage facility at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Tengah" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF Tengah">RAF Tengah</a> in Singapore between 1962 and 1971 for possible use V bombers and for Britain\'s military commitment to <a href="/wiki/SEATO" class="mw-redirect" title="SEATO">SEATO</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones2003320\xe2\x80\x93322_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones2003320\xe2\x80\x93322-217">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WE.177A_sectioned_operational_round_at_Old_Sarum-3646px.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/WE.177A_sectioned_operational_round_at_Old_Sarum-3646px.JPG/220px-WE.177A_sectioned_operational_round_at_Old_Sarum-3646px.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/WE.177A_sectioned_operational_round_at_Old_Sarum-3646px.JPG/330px-WE.177A_sectioned_operational_round_at_Old_Sarum-3646px.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/WE.177A_sectioned_operational_round_at_Old_Sarum-3646px.JPG/440px-WE.177A_sectioned_operational_round_at_Old_Sarum-3646px.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WE.177A_sectioned_operational_round_at_Old_Sarum-3646px.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rare <a href="/wiki/WE.177" title="WE.177">WE.177A</a> sectioned instructional example of an operational round</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1960 the government decided to cancel the <a href="/wiki/Blue_Streak_missile" class="mw-redirect" title="Blue Streak missile">Blue Streak missile</a> based on the Chiefs of Staff\'s conclusion that it was too vulnerable to attack and thus was only useful for a <a href="/wiki/First_strike" class="mw-redirect" title="First strike">first strike</a>, and decided to purchase the American air-launched <a href="/wiki/Skybolt_missile" class="mw-redirect" title="Skybolt missile">Skybolt missile</a> instead.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaylis1995286\xe2\x80\x93288_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaylis1995286\xe2\x80\x93288-218">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> Macmillan met with Eisenhower in March 1960, and secured permission to buy Skybolt without strings attached. In return, the Americans were given permission to base the US Navy\'s <a href="/wiki/UGM-27_Polaris" title="UGM-27 Polaris">Polaris</a>-equipped <a href="/wiki/Ballistic_missile_submarine" title="Ballistic missile submarine">ballistic missile submarines</a> at <a href="/wiki/Holy_Loch" title="Holy Loch">Holy Loch</a> in Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore201064\xe2\x80\x9368_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore201064\xe2\x80\x9368-219">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Americans initially intended to pair Skybolt with the <a href="/wiki/W47" title="W47">W47</a> warhead, an innovative light-weight design from the <a href="/wiki/Lawrence_Radiation_Laboratory" class="mw-redirect" title="Lawrence Radiation Laboratory">Lawrence Radiation Laboratory</a> developed for Polaris. The British wanted to use Red Snow, partly for safety reasons and partly because it was not certain that the advanced M47 design would be made available without strings attached. The technical problem was that Red Snow was 1,000 pounds (450&#160;kg) heavier, and therefore the range of the Skybolt would be reduced from 1,000 to 650 miles (1,610 to 1,050&#160;km). A megaton design known as RE.179 based on the <a href="/wiki/W49" title="W49">W49</a> warhead used in American ICBMs was developed for Skybolt.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2010118\xe2\x80\x93120_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2010118\xe2\x80\x93120-220">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-weapons.info_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weapons.info-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the same time, work was in progress on a Red Beard replacement for use with the RAF\'s <a href="/wiki/BAC_TSR-2" title="BAC TSR-2">BAC TSR-2</a> and the Royal Navy\'s <a href="/wiki/Blackburn_Buccaneer" title="Blackburn Buccaneer">Blackburn Buccaneer</a>. Ultimately, a warhead was produced in two variants: the high-yield (300 to 450 kilotonnes of TNT (1,300 to 1,900&#160;TJ)) WE.177B and the low-yield (0.5 or 10 kilotonnes of TNT (2.1 or 41.8&#160;TJ)) WE.177A as a Red Beard replacement, and for use in depth charges and anti-submarine missiles. WE.177 was later adapted for use with Polaris, and would become the longest-serving British nuclear weapon.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2010218\xe2\x80\x93220_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2010218\xe2\x80\x93220-222">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-weapons.info_221-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weapons.info-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The deployment of ships carrying nuclear weapons caused complications during the <a href="/wiki/Falklands_War" title="Falklands War">Falklands War</a>, and in the aftermath of that war it was decided to stockpile them ashore in peacetime.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrove1987384_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrove1987384-223">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> When the US withdrew its theatre nuclear weapons from Europe, the British government followed suit. The nuclear depth bombs were withdrawn from service in 1992,<sup id="cite_ref-weapons.info_221-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weapons.info-221">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> followed by the WE.177 free-fall bombs on 31 March 1998, and all were dismantled by the end of August.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polaris">Polaris</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Polaris">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Polaris_(UK_nuclear_programme)" title="Polaris (UK nuclear programme)">Polaris (UK nuclear programme)</a></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Kennedy_administration" class="mw-redirect" title="Kennedy administration">Kennedy administration</a> cancelled Skybolt in December 1962 because the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_Defense" title="United States Secretary of Defense">United States Secretary of Defense</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_McNamara" title="Robert McNamara">Robert McNamara</a>, determined that other delivery systems were progressing better than expected, and a further expensive system was surplus to US requirements.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2010168\xe2\x80\x93169_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2010168\xe2\x80\x93169-226">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> In London, over one hundred Conservative members of Parliament, nearly one third of the parliamentary party, signed a motion urging Macmillan to ensure that Britain remained an independent nuclear power.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones2017371_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones2017371-227">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RAF_Museum_Cosford_-_DSC08498.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/RAF_Museum_Cosford_-_DSC08498.JPG/220px-RAF_Museum_Cosford_-_DSC08498.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/RAF_Museum_Cosford_-_DSC08498.JPG/330px-RAF_Museum_Cosford_-_DSC08498.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/RAF_Museum_Cosford_-_DSC08498.JPG/440px-RAF_Museum_Cosford_-_DSC08498.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RAF_Museum_Cosford_-_DSC08498.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Polaris_missile" class="mw-redirect" title="Polaris missile">Polaris missile</a> at the <a href="/wiki/RAF_Museum_Cosford" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF Museum Cosford">RAF Museum Cosford</a> with <a href="/wiki/Chevaline" title="Chevaline">Chevaline</a> (centre, on yellow trolley)</div></div></div>\n<p>Macmillan met with President <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> and brokered the <a href="/wiki/Nassau_Agreement" title="Nassau Agreement">Nassau Agreement</a>. Macmillan rejected offers of other systems, and insisted that the UK needed to purchase Polaris <a href="/wiki/Submarine-launched_ballistic_missiles" class="mw-redirect" title="Submarine-launched ballistic missiles">submarine-launched ballistic missiles</a>. These represented more advanced technology than Skybolt, and the US was not inclined to provide them except as part of a <a href="/wiki/Multilateral_Force" title="Multilateral Force">Multilateral Force</a> within the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Treaty_Organisation" class="mw-redirect" title="North Atlantic Treaty Organisation">North Atlantic Treaty Organisation</a> (NATO).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones2017372\xe2\x80\x93379_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones2017372\xe2\x80\x93379-228">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> In the end, Kennedy did not wish to see Macmillan\'s government collapse,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison198230_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison198230-229">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> which would imperil Britain\'s entry into the <a href="/wiki/European_Economic_Community" title="European Economic Community">European Economic Community</a> (EEC),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones2017366\xe2\x80\x93368_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones2017366\xe2\x80\x93368-230">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> so a face-saving compromise was reached: the US agreed to provide the UK with Polaris missile, which would be assigned to NATO,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones2017380\xe2\x80\x93381_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones2017380\xe2\x80\x93381-231">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> and could be used independently only when "supreme national interests" intervened.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Polaris_Sales_Agreement" title="Polaris Sales Agreement">Polaris Sales Agreement</a> was signed on 6 April 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiddeke200076_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiddeke200076-233">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup> The UK retained its deterrent force, although its control passed from the RAF to the Royal Navy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDumbrell2006174_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDumbrell2006174-234">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> The Polaris missiles were equipped with British warheads.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoore2010236\xe2\x80\x93239_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoore2010236\xe2\x80\x93239-235">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones2017413\xe2\x80\x93415_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones2017413\xe2\x80\x93415-236">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> A base was developed for the Polaris submarines at <a href="/wiki/Faslane" class="mw-redirect" title="Faslane">Faslane</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Firth_of_Clyde" title="Firth of Clyde">Firth of Clyde</a>, not far from the US Navy\'s base at Holy Loch.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENailor198891,_96_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENailor198891,_96-237">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup> It was served by a weapons store at nearby <a href="/wiki/RNAD_Coulport" title="RNAD Coulport">Coulport</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrove1987243_238-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrove1987243-238">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> The first of four Polaris submarines, <a href="/wiki/HMS_Resolution_(S22)" title="HMS Resolution (S22)">HMS&#160;<i>Resolution</i></a> was launched in September 1966, and commenced its first deterrent patrol in June 1968.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELudlam2008257_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELudlam2008257-239">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> The annual running costs of the Polaris boats came to around two per cent of the defence budget, and they came to be seen as a credible deterrent that enhanced Britain\'s international status.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoddart201234_240-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoddart201234-240">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup> British politicians did not like to talk about "dependence" on the United States, preferring to describe the Special Relationship as one of "interdependence".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMiddeke200069\xe2\x80\x9370_241-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiddeke200069\xe2\x80\x9370-241">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Polaris had not been designed to penetrate ABM defences, but the Royal Navy had to ensure that its small Polaris force operating alone, and often with only one submarine on patrol, could penetrate the ABM screen around Moscow.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoddart2012153\xe2\x80\x93160_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoddart2012153\xe2\x80\x93160-242">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Second_Wilson_ministry_(United_Kingdom)" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Wilson ministry (United Kingdom)">Wilson government</a> publicly ruled out the purchase of <a href="/wiki/Poseidon_missile" class="mw-redirect" title="Poseidon missile">Poseidon missiles</a> in June 1967, and without such a commitment, the Americans were unwilling to share information about warhead vulnerability.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoddart2012128\xe2\x80\x93131_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoddart2012128\xe2\x80\x93131-243">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> The result was <a href="/wiki/Chevaline" title="Chevaline">Chevaline</a>, an Improved Front End (IFE) that replaced one of the three warheads with multiple decoys and other defensive <a href="/wiki/Countermeasure" title="Countermeasure">countermeasures</a>. in what was known as a Penetration Aid Carrier (PAC).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaylisStoddart2003128_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaylisStoddart2003128-244">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup>  It was the most technically complex defence project ever undertaken in the United Kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoddart2014a94_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoddart2014a94-245">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup> Chevaline\'s existence, along with its formerly secret codename, was revealed by the Secretary of State for Defence, <a href="/wiki/Francis_Pym" title="Francis Pym">Francis Pym</a>, during a debate in the House of Commons on 24 January 1980.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoddart2014a100\xe2\x80\x93101_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoddart2014a100\xe2\x80\x93101-246">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> By this time the project had gone on for a decade.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoddart2014b102\xe2\x80\x93105_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoddart2014b102\xe2\x80\x93105-247">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> The final cost reached  \xc2\xa31,025 million.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoddart2014b97,_105_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoddart2014b97,_105-248">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trident">Trident</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Trident">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Trident_(UK_nuclear_programme)" title="Trident (UK nuclear programme)">Trident (UK nuclear programme)</a></div>\n<p>In 1982, the <a href="/wiki/First_Thatcher_ministry" title="First Thatcher ministry">Thatcher government</a> announced its decision to purchase 65 American <a href="/wiki/UGM-133_Trident_II" title="UGM-133 Trident II">Trident II D-5 missiles</a>. These operated as part of a shared pool of weapons based at <a href="/wiki/Naval_Submarine_Base_Kings_Bay" title="Naval Submarine Base Kings Bay">Naval Submarine Base Kings Bay</a> in the United States. The US would maintain and support the missiles, while the UK would manufacture its own submarines and warheads. The warheads and missiles would be mated in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-Parliamentary_written_answers_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Parliamentary_written_answers-249">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoddart2014b197\xe2\x80\x93202_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoddart2014b197\xe2\x80\x93202-250">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> Four <a href="/wiki/Vanguard-class_submarine" title="Vanguard-class submarine"><i>Vanguard</i>-class</a> submarine were designed and built,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoddart2014b52_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoddart2014b52-251">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Victorious._08-12-2003_MOD_45143491.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/HMS_Victorious._08-12-2003_MOD_45143491.jpg/220px-HMS_Victorious._08-12-2003_MOD_45143491.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/HMS_Victorious._08-12-2003_MOD_45143491.jpg/330px-HMS_Victorious._08-12-2003_MOD_45143491.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/HMS_Victorious._08-12-2003_MOD_45143491.jpg/440px-HMS_Victorious._08-12-2003_MOD_45143491.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2500" data-file-height="1875" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HMS_Victorious._08-12-2003_MOD_45143491.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/HMS_Victorious_(S29)" title="HMS Victorious (S29)">HMS&#160;<i>Victorious</i></a>, one of four <i>Vanguard</i> class ballistic missile submarines of the Royal Navy, departs <a href="/wiki/HMNB_Clyde" title="HMNB Clyde">HMNB Clyde</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Each submarine could carry up to 16 missiles, each of which can each carry up to 8 warheads. However, when the decision to purchase Trident II was announced, it was stressed that British Trident boats would carry no more than 128 warheads\xe2\x80\x94the same number as Polaris. In November 1993, the Secretary of State for Defence, <a href="/wiki/Malcolm_Rifkind" title="Malcolm Rifkind">Malcolm Rifkind</a>, announced that each boat would deploy no more than 96 warheads.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChalmers199963_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChalmers199963-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup> In 2010 this was reduced to a maximum of 40 warheads, split between eight missiles.<sup id="cite_ref-Fact_sheet_4:_Current_system_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fact_sheet_4:_Current_system-253">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-modfeb16_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-modfeb16-254">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> The missiles have a range of 12,000 kilometres (7,500&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The first Trident boat, <a href="/wiki/HMS_Vanguard_(S28)" title="HMS Vanguard (S28)">HMS&#160;<i>Vanguard</i></a>, collected a full load of 16 missiles in 1994, but the second, <a href="/wiki/HMS_Victorious_(S29)" title="HMS Victorious (S29)">HMS&#160;<i>Victorious</i></a> drew only 12 in 1995, and the third, <a href="/wiki/HMS_Vigilant_(S30)" title="HMS Vigilant (S30)">HMS&#160;<i>Vigilant</i></a>, 14 in 1997, leaving the remaining missile tubes empty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEButlerBromley200110_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButlerBromley200110-256">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> Although the UK designed, manufactured and owns the warheads, there is evidence that the warhead design is similar to, or even based on, the US <a href="/wiki/W76" title="W76">W76</a> warhead fitted in some US Navy Trident missiles, with design data being supplied by the United States through the MDA.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since 1969 the United Kingdom has always had at least one ballistic-missile submarine on patrol, giving it a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_deterrent" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear deterrent">nuclear deterrent</a> that is, what the <a href="/wiki/Defence_Council_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="Defence Council of the United Kingdom">Defence Council</a> described in 1980 as, "effectively invulnerable to pre-emptive attack".<sup id="cite_ref-Future_Deterrent_157-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Future_Deterrent-157">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> In the Strategic Defence Review published in July 1998, the government stated that once the <i>Vanguard</i> submarines became fully operational (the fourth and final one, <a href="/wiki/HMS_Vengeance_(S31)" title="HMS Vengeance (S31)">HMS&#160;<i>Vengeance</i></a>, entered service on 27 November 1999), it would "maintain a stockpile of fewer than 200 operationally available warheads".<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> As of  2016<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, the UK had a stockpile of 215 warheads, of which 120 were operational.<sup id="cite_ref-Stockpile_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stockpile-260">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Stockpile2_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stockpile2-261">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The current Trident system cost \xc2\xa312.6 billion to build (at 1996 prices) and \xc2\xa3280m a year to maintain. Options for replacing Trident range from \xc2\xa35 billion for the missiles alone to \xc2\xa320\xe2\x80\x9330 billion for missiles, submarines and research facilities. At minimum, for the system to continue after around 2020, the missiles will need to be replaced.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> The price of replacement of submarine has risen to \xc2\xa331 billion and it is estimated by Ministry of Defence that the cost of Trident replacement program for 30 years to be at \xc2\xa3167 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trident_renewal">Trident renewal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Trident renewal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Trident_nuclear_programme#Trident_renewal" class="mw-redirect" title="Trident nuclear programme">Trident nuclear programme \xc2\xa7&#160;Trident renewal</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HMNB_Clyde.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/HMNB_Clyde.jpg/170px-HMNB_Clyde.jpg" width="170" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/HMNB_Clyde.jpg/255px-HMNB_Clyde.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/HMNB_Clyde.jpg/340px-HMNB_Clyde.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="792" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HMNB_Clyde.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Faslane" class="mw-redirect" title="Faslane">Faslane</a> Naval Base, home of the <a href="/wiki/Vanguard-class_submarine" title="Vanguard-class submarine"><i>Vanguard</i>-class submarines</a> which carry the UK\'s nuclear arsenal.</div></div></div>\n<p>With the tactical nuclear weapons had withdrawn from service, Trident was the UK\'s only remaining nuclear weapons system.<sup id="cite_ref-Select_Committee_Eighth_Report_264-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Select_Committee_Eighth_Report-264">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup> By this time, possession of nuclear weapons had become an important part of Brititain\'s national identity. Not renewing Trident meant that Britain would become an non-nuclear power. It would spell the end of the Special Relationship with the United States,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERitchie20087\xe2\x80\x939_265-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERitchie20087\xe2\x80\x939-265">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup> and strike at Britain\'s self-image as a great power.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERitchie20081\xe2\x80\x935_266-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERitchie20081\xe2\x80\x935-266">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup> A decision on the renewal of Trident was made on 4 December 2006. Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> told MPs it would be "unwise and dangerous" for the UK to give up its nuclear weapons. He outlined plans to spend up to \xc2\xa320bn on a new generation of ballistic missile submarines. The new boars would continue to carry the Trident II D-5 missiles, but submarine numbers might be cut from four to three, and the number of nuclear warheads would be cut by 20% to 160. He said although the Cold War had ended, the UK needed nuclear weapons, as no-one could be sure another nuclear threat would not emerge in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-Blair_267-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blair-267">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/2010_United_Kingdom_government_formation" title="2010 United Kingdom government formation">2010 coalition government</a> agreed "that the renewal of Trident should be scrutinised to ensure value for money. Liberal Democrats will continue to make the case for alternatives." Research and development work continued, but the final decision to proceed with  building a replacement was scheduled for 2016, after the next election.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> There was already some urgency to move ahead because some experts predicted it could take 17 years to develop the replacement for the <i>Vanguard</i>-class submarines.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The vote in the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_the_United_Kingdom" title="House of Commons of the United Kingdom">House of Commons</a> on whether to replace the existing four <i>Vanguard</i>-class submarines was held on 18 July 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> The Trident renewal programme motion passed with a significant majority with 472 MPs voting in favour and 117 against. The <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Opposition_(United_Kingdom)" title="Leader of the Opposition (United Kingdom)">Leader of the Opposition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Corbyn" title="Jeremy Corbyn">Jeremy Corbyn</a>, and 47 other Labour MPs had voted against it; 41 did not vote but 140 Labour votes were cast in favour of the motion.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> The four new <a href="/wiki/Dreadnought-class_submarine" title="Dreadnought-class submarine"><i>Dreadnought</i>-class</a> submarines were expected to come into operation in the early 2030s,<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> with the programme lasting until at least the 2060s.<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_tests">Nuclear tests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Nuclear tests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons_tests_in_Australia" title="Nuclear weapons tests in Australia">Nuclear weapons tests in Australia</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_nuclear_testing_in_the_United_States" title="British nuclear testing in the United States">British nuclear testing in the United States</a></div>\n<p>The UK\'s first nuclear test, Operation Hurricane, occurred was in the Montebello Islands of Western Australia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldberg1964409\xe2\x80\x93429_87-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldberg1964409\xe2\x80\x93429-87">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> It was followed by the first nuclear tests on the Australian mainland, which were conducted at <a href="/wiki/Emu_Field" class="mw-redirect" title="Emu Field">Emu Field</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Great_Victoria_Desert" title="Great Victoria Desert">Great Victoria Desert</a> in <a href="/wiki/South_Australia" title="South Australia">South Australia</a> as part of <a href="/wiki/Operation_Totem" title="Operation Totem">Operation Totem</a> on 14 and 26 October 1953.<sup id="cite_ref-modkey_278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-modkey-278">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup> Two further tests were held on the Montebello Islands as part of <a href="/wiki/Operation_Mosaic" title="Operation Mosaic">Operation Mosaic</a> on 6 May and 19 June 1956.<sup id="cite_ref-modkey_278-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-modkey-278">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup> In the 1980s there emerged a claim that the second Mosaic test was of a significantly higher explosive yield than suggested by available figures\xe2\x80\x9498 kilotonnes of TNT (410&#160;TJ) as compared to the official figure of 60 kilotonnes of TNT (250&#160;TJ)\xe2\x80\x94but this claim does not  stand up to scrutiny.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELeonard2014210\xe2\x80\x93214_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeonard2014210\xe2\x80\x93214-279">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Totem_One_Obelisk.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Totem_One_Obelisk.jpg/220px-Totem_One_Obelisk.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Totem_One_Obelisk.jpg/330px-Totem_One_Obelisk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Totem_One_Obelisk.jpg/440px-Totem_One_Obelisk.jpg 2x" data-file-width="656" data-file-height="468" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div
