class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Totem_One_Obelisk.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Site of Totem 1, the first nuclear test on the Australian mainland</div></div></div>\n<p>The British government formally requested a permanent test facility on 30 October 1953. Due to concerns about <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fallout" title="Nuclear fallout">nuclear fallout</a> from the previous tests at Emu Field and the site\'s inadequate infrastructure and water supply, the recently surveyed site  at <a href="/wiki/Maralinga,_South_Australia" class="mw-redirect" title="Maralinga, South Australia">Maralinga</a> in South Australia was selected for this purpose.<sup id="cite_ref-fedmeteor_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fedmeteor-280">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup> The new site was announced in May 1955.<sup id="cite_ref-modkey_278-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-modkey-278">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-dateuncertainty_281-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dateuncertainty-281">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup> It was developed as a joint, co-funded facility between the British and Australian governments.<sup id="cite_ref-natarchive_282-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-natarchive-282">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup> Seven <a href="/wiki/British_nuclear_tests_at_Maralinga" title="British nuclear tests at Maralinga">British nuclear tests at Maralinga</a> were conducted between 27 September 1956 and 9 October 1957.<sup id="cite_ref-modkey_278-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-modkey-278">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition to the major tests involving explosions, many <a href="/wiki/Subcritical_testing" class="mw-redirect" title="Subcritical testing">subcritical</a> minor trials were also carried out between June 1955 and April 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-modkey_278-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-modkey-278">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup> While the major tests had been carried out with some publicity, the minor tests were carried out in absolute secrecy.<sup id="cite_ref-keane_283-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-keane-283">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup> The "Kitten" tests tested bomb components, while "Tims" and "Rats" were early subcritical hydronuclear tests.  The "Vixen" tests involved safety testing of nuclear weapons\xe2\x80\x94assuring that the core would not accidentally undergo criticality in the event of a fire or crash.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnoldSmith2006215\xe2\x80\x93225_284-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnoldSmith2006215\xe2\x80\x93225-284">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> These minor tests left a legacy of radioactive contamination at Maralinga.<sup id="cite_ref-radwaste_285-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-radwaste-285">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-green_286-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-green-286">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Australian government prohibited hydrogen bomb tests in Australia, so Britain had to look for another test site for its hydrogen bombs.<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> The first British hydrogen bombs were tested during Operation Grapple at Malden Island and <a href="/wiki/Kiritimati" title="Kiritimati">Christmas Island</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific Ocean</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne200196\xe2\x80\x93102_289-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne200196\xe2\x80\x93102-289">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup> Nine tests were conducted there in 1957, 1958 and 1959,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001236_290-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001236-290">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup> ultimately demonstrating that the UK had developed expertise in thermonuclear weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001195\xe2\x80\x93196_291-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne2001195\xe2\x80\x93196-291">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Beginning in December 1962, the UK conducted 24 tests at the <a href="/wiki/Nevada_Test_Site" title="Nevada Test Site">Nevada Test Site</a> in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Database_292-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Database-292">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup> The final test was the <a href="/wiki/Julin_Bristol" class="mw-redirect" title="Julin Bristol">Julin Bristol</a> shot which took place on 26 November 1991.<sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> British nuclear testing was abruptly halted by President <a href="/wiki/George_H._W._Bush" title="George H. W. Bush">George H. W. Bush</a> in October 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaylis2008462_294-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaylis2008462-294">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWade2008209_295-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWade2008209-295">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup>  Because Britain did not test as often as the United States for financial and political reasons, and did not have the Americans\' state-of-the-art computer facilities, British weapons design depended more on theoretical understanding, with potential for both greater advances and greater risks between tests.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESpinardi1997547\xe2\x80\x93582_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpinardi1997547\xe2\x80\x93582-103">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The United Kingdom, along with the United States and the Soviet Union, signed the <a href="/wiki/Partial_Test_Ban_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Partial Test Ban Treaty">Partial Test Ban Treaty</a>, which restricted it to underground nuclear tests by outlawing testing in the atmosphere, underwater, or in outer space, on 5 August 1963.<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup> The UK signed the <a href="/wiki/Comprehensive_Test_Ban_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Comprehensive Test Ban Treaty">Comprehensive Test Ban Treaty</a>, ending all nuclear testing, on 24 September 1996,<sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup> and ratified it on 6 April 1998,<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup> having passed the necessary legislation on 18 March 1998 as the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Explosions_(Prohibition_and_Inspections)_Act_1998" title="Nuclear Explosions (Prohibition and Inspections) Act 1998">Nuclear Explosions (Prohibition and Inspections) Act 1998</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> Subcritical nuclear tests continued to occur, most notably the Etna test in February 2002, and the Krakatau test in February 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWade2008210_300-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWade2008210-300">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="US_nuclear_weapons_in_the_UK">US nuclear weapons in the UK</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: US nuclear weapons in the UK">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="United States Air Force in the United Kingdom">United States Air Force in the United Kingdom</a> and <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Air_Command_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Strategic Air Command in the United Kingdom">Strategic Air Command in the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<p>In the early years of the Cold War, the majority of the bomber force of the US <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Air_Command" title="Strategic Air Command">Strategic Air Command</a> (SAC) was made of <a href="/wiki/Boeing_B-29_Superfortress" title="Boeing B-29 Superfortress">Boeing B-29 Superfortress</a> bombers, and later its successors, the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_B-50_Superfortress" title="Boeing B-50 Superfortress">Boeing B-50 Superfortress</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Boeing_B-47_Stratojet" title="Boeing B-47 Stratojet">Boeing B-47 Stratojet</a>, all of which lacked the necessary range to reach targets in the Soviet Union from bases in the continental United States. Even small number of <a href="/wiki/Convair_B-36_Peacemaker" title="Convair B-36 Peacemaker">Convair B-36 Peacemaker</a> bombers could not make it there and back. Overseas bases were therefore required, and the need for bases in the UK was a feature of American war planning for over a decade.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung2007118\xe2\x80\x93119_301-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung2007118\xe2\x80\x93119-301">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:F-15E_Strike_Eagle.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/F-15E_Strike_Eagle.jpg/220px-F-15E_Strike_Eagle.jpg" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/F-15E_Strike_Eagle.jpg/330px-F-15E_Strike_Eagle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/F-15E_Strike_Eagle.jpg/440px-F-15E_Strike_Eagle.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="641" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:F-15E_Strike_Eagle.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>USAF F-15E of <a href="/wiki/494th_Fighter_Squadron" title="494th Fighter Squadron">494th Fighter Squadron</a> stationed at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Lakenheath" title="RAF Lakenheath">RAF Lakenheath</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Obtaining British permission was easy thanks to the wartime comradeship between the RAF and the United States Army Air Forces (USAAF). Bypassing the politicians, <a href="/wiki/General_(United_States)" title="General (United States)">General</a> <a href="/wiki/Carl_Spaatz" title="Carl Spaatz">Carl Spaatz</a>, the commander of the USAAF, came to an agreement with the Chief Of the Air Staff, Marshal of the Royal Air Force Lord Tedder, in June and July 1946. Work began on extended and strengtheners runways at RAF airbases in East Anglia to receive the B-29s. In June 1947, nine B-29s of the <a href="/wiki/97th_Bombardment_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="97th Bombardment Group">97th Bombardment Group</a> deployed to RAF Marham, where they were greeted by Tedder.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung2007120\xe2\x80\x93122_302-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung2007120\xe2\x80\x93122-302">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> This was merely a test; the bombers were not nuclear-capable. Only the <a href="/wiki/Silverplate" title="Silverplate">Silverplate</a> B-29s of the <a href="/wiki/509th_Bombardment_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="509th Bombardment Group">509th Bombardment Group</a> could do so.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung2007123\xe2\x80\x93124_303-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung2007123\xe2\x80\x93124-303">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup> Their first deployment was in April 1949.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung2007130_304-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung2007130-304">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup> Ninety sets of bomb assemblies\xe2\x80\x94atomic bombs without the fissile cores\xe2\x80\x94were stored in the UK by July 1950, and authority to deploy the cores as well was given in April 1954.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung2007133_305-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung2007133-305">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/3d_Air_Division" title="3d Air Division">3d Air Division</a> was formed in 1949 to control the deployments of B-29s to the UK. It was soon upgraded to the status of a major command, and became the <a href="/wiki/Third_Air_Force" title="Third Air Force">Third Air Force</a> in May 1951 as part of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Forces_in_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Air Forces in Europe">United States Air Forces in Europe</a>. SAC then formed the <a href="/wiki/7th_Air_Division" title="7th Air Division">7th Air Division</a> to control the nuclear bomber deployments.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYoung200758_306-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYoung200758-306">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup> In fulfilment of NATO\'s plans to halt a Soviet invasion of Western Europe using tactical nuclear weapons, the 3rd Air Force received its own nuclear weapons when the <a href="/wiki/20th_Fighter_Wing" title="20th Fighter Wing">20th Fighter Wing</a> deployed to <a href="/wiki/RAF_Wethersfield" class="mw-redirect" title="RAF Wethersfield">RAF Wethersfield</a> in Essex on 1 June 1952, with <a href="/wiki/Republic_F-84F_Thunderstreak" title="Republic F-84F Thunderstreak">Republic F-84F Thunderstreak</a> fighters and Mark 7 nuclear bombs. It reequipped with the <a href="/wiki/North_American_F-100_Super_Sabre" title="North American F-100 Super Sabre">North American F-100 Super Sabre</a> in 1957, and the <a href="/wiki/General_Dynamics_F-111" class="mw-redirect" title="General Dynamics F-111">General Dynamics F-111</a> in 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrant201158\xe2\x80\x9362_307-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrant201158\xe2\x80\x9362-307">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup> The US Navy\'s Polaris ballistic missile submarines arrived at Holy Loch in Scotland in March 1961.<sup id="cite_ref-Cold_War&#39;s_End_309-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cold_War&#39;s_End-309">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>With the introduction to service of long-range bombers and <a href="/wiki/Intercontinental_ballistic_missile" title="Intercontinental ballistic missile">intercontinental ballistic missiles</a>, the need for a SAC presence in the UK diminished, On 3 April 1964, the last SAC aircraft, a B-47 from the <a href="/wiki/380th_Bombardment_Wing" class="mw-redirect" title="380th Bombardment Wing">380th Bombardment Wing</a>, left <a href="/wiki/RAF_Brize_Norton" title="RAF Brize Norton">RAF Brize Norton</a>, ending nearly 12 years\' of continual B-47 deployments, and the 7th Air Division on 30 June 1964.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWynn1997626\xe2\x80\x93627_310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWynn1997626\xe2\x80\x93627-310">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup> The tactical nuclear weapons remained. In the 1970s, up to 60 F-111s based in the UK were on quick reaction alert, each carrying multiple <a href="/wiki/B61_nuclear_bomb" title="B61 nuclear bomb">B61 nuclear bombs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGrant201158\xe2\x80\x9362_307-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGrant201158\xe2\x80\x9362-307">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup> About 70 F-111s were based in the UK from 1971. In 1977, the Labour government gave permission for another 90 to be deployed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreedman1999133_311-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreedman1999133-311">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup> During the 1980s nuclear armed USAF <a href="/wiki/Ground_Launched_Cruise_Missile" class="mw-redirect" title="Ground Launched Cruise Missile">Ground Launched Cruise Missiles</a> (GLCMs) were deployed at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Greenham_Common" title="RAF Greenham Common">RAF Greenham Common</a> and <a href="/wiki/RAF_Molesworth" title="RAF Molesworth">RAF Molesworth</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoddart2014b236_312-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoddart2014b236-312">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup> as a consequence of the 1979 <a href="/wiki/NATO_Double-Track_Decision" title="NATO Double-Track Decision">NATO Double-Track Decision</a>, under which NATO countries agreed to modernise the alliance\'s nuclear weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoddart2014b211\xe2\x80\x93217_313-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoddart2014b211\xe2\x80\x93217-313">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Under the terms of the 1987 <a href="/wiki/Intermediate-Range_Nuclear_Forces_Treaty" title="Intermediate-Range Nuclear Forces Treaty">Intermediate-Range Nuclear Forces Treaty</a> with the Soviet Union, the United States withdrew its surface naval nuclear weapons and short-range nuclear forces. The GLCMs were withdrawn from the UK in 1991,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERitchie201414\xe2\x80\x9316_211-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERitchie201414\xe2\x80\x9316-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> and the Polaris submarine base at Holy Loch was closed in 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChalmers199963_252-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChalmers199963-252">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Cold_War&#39;s_End_309-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cold_War&#39;s_End-309">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup> The US continued to store tactical nuclear weapons in the UK until 2006, when approximately 110 tactical B61s stored at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Lakenheath" title="RAF Lakenheath">RAF Lakenheath</a> for deployment by USAF <a href="/wiki/F-15E_Strike_Eagle" class="mw-redirect" title="F-15E Strike Eagle">F-15E Strike Eagle</a> aircraft were removed.<sup id="cite_ref-nrdc_314-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nrdc-314">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERitchie201414\xe2\x80\x9316_211-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERitchie201414\xe2\x80\x9316-211">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_defence">Nuclear defence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Nuclear defence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Britain was extremely vulnerable to nuclear weapons. The 1955 <a href="/wiki/Strath_Committee" title="Strath Committee">Strath Committee</a> grimly estimated that an attack on the UK with just ten 10-megaton weapons would kill 12 million people and seriously injure another 4 million even before country was blanketed with radioactive fallout.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESelf2010201_316-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESelf2010201-316">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Warning_systems">Warning systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Warning systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Radar_RAF_Fylingdales.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Radar_RAF_Fylingdales.jpg/220px-Radar_RAF_Fylingdales.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Radar_RAF_Fylingdales.jpg/330px-Radar_RAF_Fylingdales.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Radar_RAF_Fylingdales.jpg/440px-Radar_RAF_Fylingdales.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3211" data-file-height="2200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Radar_RAF_Fylingdales.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This solid-state <a href="/wiki/Phased_array" title="Phased array">phased array</a> <a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">radar</a> at RAF Fylingdales in <a href="/wiki/North_Yorkshire" title="North Yorkshire">North Yorkshire</a> is a UK-controlled early warning station and part of the American-controlled <a href="/wiki/Ballistic_Missile_Early_Warning_System" title="Ballistic Missile Early Warning System">Ballistic Missile Early Warning System</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Four-minute_warning" title="Four-minute warning">Four-minute warning</a>, <a href="/wiki/RAF_Fylingdales" title="RAF Fylingdales">RAF Fylingdales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ballistic_Missile_Early_Warning_System" title="Ballistic Missile Early Warning System">Ballistic Missile Early Warning System</a>, and <a href="/wiki/National_Missile_Defense" class="mw-redirect" title="National Missile Defense">National Missile Defense</a></div>\n<p>The UK has relied on the <a href="/wiki/Ballistic_Missile_Early_Warning_System" title="Ballistic Missile Early Warning System">Ballistic Missile Early Warning System</a> (BMEWS) and, in later years, <a href="/wiki/Defense_Support_Program" title="Defense Support Program">Defense Support Program</a> (DSP) satellites for warning of a nuclear attack. Both of these systems are owned and controlled by the United States, although the UK has joint control over UK-based systems. One of the four component radars for the BMEWS is based at <a href="/wiki/RAF_Fylingdales" title="RAF Fylingdales">RAF Fylingdales</a> in <a href="/wiki/North_Yorkshire" title="North Yorkshire">North Yorkshire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStocker200413\xe2\x80\x9316_317-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStocker200413\xe2\x80\x9316-317">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 2003 the UK government stated that it would consent to a request from the US to upgrade the radar at Fylingdales for use in the US <a href="/wiki/National_Missile_Defense" class="mw-redirect" title="National Missile Defense">National Missile Defense</a> system,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStocker200413\xe2\x80\x9316_317-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStocker200413\xe2\x80\x9316-317">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">&#91;319&#93;</a></sup> but missile defence was not a significant political issue within the UK.  The ballistic missile threat was perceived to be less severe, and consequently less of a priority, than other threats to its security.<sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">&#91;320&#93;</a></sup> Fylingdales was enhanced to an <a href="/wiki/Upgraded_Early_Warning_Radar" class="mw-redirect" title="Upgraded Early Warning Radar">Upgraded Early Warning Radar</a> (UEWR) in 2008, and became part of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_national_missile_defense" title="United States national missile defense">United States national missile defense</a> system in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attack_scenarios">Attack scenarios</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Attack scenarios">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>During the Cold War, a significant effort by government and academia was made to assess the effects of a nuclear attack on the UK. There were four major exercises:\n</p>\n<ul><li><b>Exercise <i>Inside Right</i></b> took place in 1975.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell198222_322-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell198222-322">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Exercise <i>Scrum Half</i></b> was conducted in 1978.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell198222_322-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell198222-322">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Exercise <i><a href="/wiki/Square_Leg" title="Square Leg">Square Leg</a></i></b> was conducted in 1980.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell198222_322-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell198222-322">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup> The scenario involved around 130 warheads with a total yield of 205 <a href="/wiki/Megatons" class="mw-redirect" title="Megatons">megatons</a> (69 <a href="/wiki/Ground_burst" title="Ground burst">ground burst</a>, 62 <a href="/wiki/Air_burst" title="Air burst">air burst</a>) with an average of 1.5 megatons per bomb. The exercise was criticised as unrealistic as an actual exchange would be much larger, and did not include targets in <a href="/wiki/Inner_London" title="Inner London">Inner London</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Whitehall" title="Whitehall">Whitehall</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOpenshawSteadmanGreene1983105_323-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOpenshawSteadmanGreene1983105-323">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup> Even so, the effect of the limited attack in Square Leg was estimated to be 29 million dead (53 per cent of the population) and 6.4 million seriously injured.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell198253\xe2\x80\x9354_324-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell198253\xe2\x80\x9354-324">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Exercise <i>Hard Rock</i></b> was a combined communications and civil defence exercise planned for September and October 1982. It assumed a conventional war in Europe lasting two to three days, during which the UK would be attacked with conventional weapons, then a limited nuclear exchange, with 54 nuclear warheads used against military targets in the UK. 250,000 people protested against the exercise and 24 councils refused to participate. The limited scenario still assumed casualties of 7.9 million dead and 5 million injured.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell198253\xe2\x80\x9354_324-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell198253\xe2\x80\x9354-324">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup> The scenario was ridiculed by the <a href="/wiki/Campaign_for_Nuclear_Disarmament" title="Campaign for Nuclear Disarmament">Campaign for Nuclear Disarmament</a> and the exercise was postponed indefinitely.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWittner2003294_325-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWittner2003294-325">&#91;325&#93;</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/New_Statesman" title="New Statesman">New Statesman</a></i> later claimed the Ministry of Defence insisted on having a veto over proposed targets in the exercise and several were removed to make them politically more acceptable; for example, the nuclear submarine base at Faslane was removed from the target list.<sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_defence">Civil defence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Civil defence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Civil_defence" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil defence">Civil defence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protect_and_Survive" title="Protect and Survive">Protect and Survive</a></div>\n<p>Succesive governments developed <a href="/wiki/Civil_defence" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil defence">civil defence</a> programmes aimed to prepare civilian and local government infrastructure for a nuclear strike on the UK. A series of seven <a href="/wiki/Civil_Defence_Bulletin" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil Defence Bulletin">Civil Defence Bulletin</a> films were produced in 1964,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECampbell1982124\xe2\x80\x93130_327-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECampbell1982124\xe2\x80\x93130-327">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup> and in the 1980s the most famous such programme was probably the series of booklets and <a href="/wiki/Public_information_film" title="Public information film">public information films</a> entitled <i><a href="/wiki/Protect_and_Survive" title="Protect and Survive">Protect and Survive</a></i>.\n</p>\n<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>If the country was ever faced with an immediate threat of nuclear threat or complete annihilation, a copy of this booklet would be distributed to every household as part of a public information campaign which would include announcements on television and radio and in the press. The booklet has been designed for free and general distribution in that event. It is being placed on sale now for those who wish to know what they would be advised to do at such a time.<sup id="cite_ref-Protect_and_Survive_328-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Protect_and_Survive-328">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The booklet contained information on building a <a href="/wiki/Fallout_shelter" title="Fallout shelter">nuclear refuge</a> within a so-called "fall-out room" at home, sanitation, limiting fire hazards, and descriptions of the audio signals for attack warning, fall-out warning and all clear. It was anticipated that families might need to stay in their fall-out room for up to 14 days after an attack almost without leaving it at all.<sup id="cite_ref-Protect_and_Survive_328-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Protect_and_Survive-328">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup> The government also prepared a recorded announcement which was to have been broadcast by the <a href="/wiki/British_Broadcasting_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="British Broadcasting Corporation">BBC</a> if a nuclear attack ever did occur.<sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">&#91;329&#93;</a></sup> Sirens left over from the <a href="/wiki/London_Blitz" class="mw-redirect" title="London Blitz">London Blitz</a> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> were also to be used to warn the public. The system was mostly dismantled in 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research_and_production_facilities">Research and production facilities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Research and production facilities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Atomic_Weapons_Establishment" title="Atomic Weapons Establishment">Atomic Weapons Establishment</a>, <a href="/wiki/ROF_Burghfield" class="mw-redirect" title="ROF Burghfield">ROF Burghfield</a>, and <a href="/wiki/ROF_Cardiff" class="mw-redirect" title="ROF Cardiff">ROF Cardiff</a></div>\n<p>The Atomic Weapons Establishment (AWE), Aldermaston, formerly the Atomic Weapons Research Establishment (AWRE), is situated on a 750-acre (300&#160;ha)  near <a href="/wiki/Reading,_Berkshire" title="Reading, Berkshire">Reading</a> in Berkshire.<sup id="cite_ref-331" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-331">&#91;331&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Administration_of_Aldermaston_332-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Administration_of_Aldermaston-332">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup> It was built on the site of the former <a href="/wiki/RAF_Aldermaston" title="RAF Aldermaston">RAF Aldermaston</a>,  which was converted to nuclear weapons research, design and development establishment, and opened on 1 April 1950. In 1954 the AWRE took control of the nearby 225-acre (91&#160;ha) <a href="/wiki/ROF_Burghfield" class="mw-redirect" title="ROF Burghfield">ROF Burghfield</a>, where warheads were assembled, and the test ranges at <a href="/wiki/Foulness" class="mw-redirect" title="Foulness">Foulness</a> and <a href="/wiki/Orford_Ness" title="Orford Ness">Orford Ness</a>. Components for nuclear weapons were also produced at the former <a href="/wiki/ROF_Cardiff" class="mw-redirect" title="ROF Cardiff">ROF Cardiff</a> site.<sup id="cite_ref-Administration_of_Aldermaston_332-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Administration_of_Aldermaston-332">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Aldermaston_history_333-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aldermaston_history-333">&#91;333&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Atomic Weapons Research Establishment became part of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_Atomic_Energy_Authority" title="United Kingdom Atomic Energy Authority">United Kingdom Atomic Energy Authority</a> on 1 January 1955.<sup id="cite_ref-Aldermaston_history_333-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aldermaston_history-333">&#91;333&#93;</a></sup> The last trials at Orford Ness were conducted on 9 June 1971, and the site was closed on 1 October 1971.<sup id="cite_ref-334" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-334">&#91;334&#93;</a></sup>\nCardiff closed in 1997,<sup id="cite_ref-Administration_of_Aldermaston_332-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Administration_of_Aldermaston-332">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup> and Foulness by the end of that year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECocroftNewsome200925_335-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECocroftNewsome200925-335">&#91;335&#93;</a></sup> In 1989, the government announced its intention to find a private company to run AWE, with the government retaining ownership of the site and control of AWE though a <a href="/wiki/Golden_share" title="Golden share">golden share</a> arrangement. In 1993, the contract was awarded to a consortium of <a href="/wiki/Hunting_Engineering" class="mw-redirect" title="Hunting Engineering">Hunting Engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Brown_and_Root" class="mw-redirect" title="Brown and Root">Brown and Root</a> and <a href="/wiki/AEA_Technology" title="AEA Technology">AEA Technology</a>. In 1999, the contract was transferred to a consortium of <a href="/wiki/BNFL" class="mw-redirect" title="BNFL">BNFL</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_Martin" title="Lockheed Martin">Lockheed Martin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Serco" title="Serco">Serco</a>. In 2008, the British government sold the BNFL share to <a href="/wiki/Jacobs_Engineering_Group" title="Jacobs Engineering Group">Jacobs Engineering Group</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Administration_of_Aldermaston_332-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Administration_of_Aldermaston-332">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Nuclear warheads from the Trident missiles are transported by road convoy several times a year from Coulport to Burghfield for refurbishment. Between 2000 and 2016 there were 180 accidents involving the vehicles, ranging from minor traffic accidents to a sudden total loss of power in one of the 44-tonne lorries that halted a convoy and caused a double lane closure and a <a href="/wiki/Traffic_congestion" title="Traffic congestion">tailback</a> on the <a href="/wiki/M6_motorway" title="M6 motorway">M6 motorway</a>. The accidents have been more frequent in recent years.<sup id="cite_ref-336" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-336">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-nuclear_movement">Anti-nuclear movement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Anti-nuclear movement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear_movement_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Anti-nuclear movement in the United Kingdom">Anti-nuclear movement in the United Kingdom</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Peace_symbol.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Peace_symbol.svg/170px-Peace_symbol.svg.png" width="170" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Peace_symbol.svg/255px-Peace_symbol.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Peace_symbol.svg/340px-Peace_symbol.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="512" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Peace_symbol.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The now-familiar <a href="/wiki/Peace_symbol" class="mw-redirect" title="Peace symbol">peace symbol</a> was originally the <a href="/wiki/Campaign_for_Nuclear_Disarmament" title="Campaign for Nuclear Disarmament">Campaign for Nuclear Disarmament</a> logo</div></div></div>\n<p>The anti-nuclear movement in the United Kingdom consists of groups who oppose nuclear technologies such as <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear weapons">nuclear weapons</a>. Many different groups and individuals have been involved in <a href="/wiki/Anti-nuclear_protests" title="Anti-nuclear protests">anti-nuclear demonstrations and protests</a> over the years. One of the most prominent anti-nuclear groups in the UK is the Campaign for Nuclear Disarmament (CND). This national movement was founded in the late 1950s, initially in opposition to nuclear testing. It reached its peak around 1960, by which time it had evolved into a broader movement calling for Britain to unilaterally give up nuclear weapons, withdraw from NATO, and end the basing of US bombers armed with nuclear weapons in the UK.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBowiePlatt198463\xe2\x80\x9370_337-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowiePlatt198463\xe2\x80\x9370-337">&#91;337&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The end of atmospheric nuclear testing, internal squabbles, and activists focusing their energies on other causes led to a rapid decline, but it revived in the early 1980s in the wake of the Thatcher government\'s December 1979 decision to deploy US GLCMs in the UK, and the announcement of its decision to purchase Trident in July 1980. Membership leapt from 3,000 in 1980 to 50,000 a year later, and rallies for unilateral nuclear disarmament in London in October 1981 and June 1982 attracted 250,000 marchers, the largest ever mass demonstrations in the UK up to that time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBowiePlatt198463\xe2\x80\x9370_337-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBowiePlatt198463\xe2\x80\x9370-337">&#91;337&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_cross-party_support">End of cross-party support</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: End of cross-party support">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>There was little dissent in the House of Commons; nuclear weapons had almost bipartisan support until 1960, with only the <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Party_(UK)" title="Liberal Party (UK)">Liberals</a> dissenting in 1958. Despite opposition from its left wing the Labour party supported British nuclear weapons but opposed tests, and Labour Opposition Leader <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Gaitskell" title="Hugh Gaitskell">Hugh Gaitskell</a> and shadow foreign secretary <a href="/wiki/Aneurin_Bevan" title="Aneurin Bevan">Aneurin Bevan</a> agreed with Sandys on the importance of reducing dependence on the American deterrent. Bevan told his colleagues that their demand for <a href="/wiki/Unilateral_nuclear_disarmament" class="mw-redirect" title="Unilateral nuclear disarmament">unilateral nuclear disarmament</a> would send a future Labour government "naked into the conference chamber" during international negotiations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEpstein1966145_338-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEpstein1966145-338">&#91;338&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jeremy_Corbyn_Says_-StopTrident_-_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Jeremy_Corbyn_Says_-StopTrident_-_2.jpg/170px-Jeremy_Corbyn_Says_-StopTrident_-_2.jpg" width="170" height="226" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Jeremy_Corbyn_Says_-StopTrident_-_2.jpg/255px-Jeremy_Corbyn_Says_-StopTrident_-_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/07/Jeremy_Corbyn_Says_-StopTrident_-_2.jpg/340px-Jeremy_Corbyn_Says_-StopTrident_-_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="798" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jeremy_Corbyn_Says_-StopTrident_-_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Opposition_(United_Kingdom)" title="Leader of the Opposition (United Kingdom)">Leader of the Opposition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Corbyn" title="Jeremy Corbyn">Jeremy Corbyn</a> speaks at the #StopTrident rally at <a href="/wiki/Trafalgar_Square" title="Trafalgar Square">Trafalgar Square</a> on 27 February 2016</div></div></div>\n<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Manchester_Guardian" class="mw-redirect" title="Manchester Guardian">Manchester Guardian</a></i> and other newspapers critical of the Conservative government supported the British deterrent, although the <i>Guardian</i> did criticise the government for relying on developing bombers rather than missiles to carry the weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne200165_339-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnoldPyne200165-339">&#91;339&#93;</a></sup> In 1962 it stated that the forthcoming <a href="/wiki/Chinese_nuclear_weapon" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese nuclear weapon">Chinese nuclear weapon</a> was a reason for having more than one Western nuclear nation. From 1955 the government chose to emphasize the nuclear deterrent and de-emphasize conventional forces.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEpstein1966140_340-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEpstein1966140-340">&#91;340&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Economist" title="The Economist">The Economist</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/New_Statesman" title="New Statesman">New Statesman</a></i>, and many left-wing newspapers supported the reliance on nuclear deterrence and nuclear weapons, but in their view considered that of the United States would suffice, and that of the costs of the "nuclear umbrella" was best left to be borne by the United States alone.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGroom1974131\xe2\x80\x93154_341-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGroom1974131\xe2\x80\x93154-341">&#91;341&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Gaitskell\'s Labour party ceased supporting an independent deterrent in 1960 via its new "Policy for Peace", after the cancellation of Blue Streak made nuclear independence less likely. Labour also adopted a resolution favoring unilateral disarmament. Although Gaitskell opposed the resolution and it was reversed in 1961 in favor of continuing support of a general Western nuclear deterrent, the party\'s opposition to a British deterrent remained and became more prominent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEpstein1966146\xe2\x80\x93156_342-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEpstein1966146\xe2\x80\x93156-342">&#91;342&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>This became a campaign issue during the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1964" title="United Kingdom general election, 1964">1964 general election</a>. <a href="/wiki/Alec_Douglas-Home" title="Alec Douglas-Home">Alec Douglas-Home</a>\'s incumbent Conservatives stated that the British deterrent was both necessary for independence from the Americans and maintaining British world influence, and was "working for peace" in such cases as the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Test_Ban_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear Test Ban Treaty">Nuclear Test Ban Treaty</a>. Led by Gaitskell\'s successor <a href="/wiki/Harold_Wilson" title="Harold Wilson">Harold Wilson</a>, Labour emphasized domestic economic issues but called deterrence the "Tory Nuclear Pretense" which would be neither independent nor effective. The populace\'s greater interest in domestic over foreign policy likely contributed to Labour\'s victory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEpstein1966146\xe2\x80\x93156_342-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEpstein1966146\xe2\x80\x93156-342">&#91;342&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 1982 <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_Conference" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour Party Conference">Labour Party Conference</a> adopted a platform calling for the removal of the GLCMs, the scrapping of Polaris and the cancellation of Trident. This was reaffirmed by the 1986 conference. While the party was given little chance of winning the 1983 election in the aftermath of the Falklands War, polls had shown Labour ahead of the Conservatives in 1986 and 1987. In the wake of Labour\'s unsuccessful performance in the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_general_election,_1987" title="United Kingdom general election, 1987">1987 election</a>, the Labour Party leader, <a href="/wiki/Neil_Kinnock" title="Neil Kinnock">Neil Kinnock</a>, despite his own unilateralist convictions, moved to drop the party\'s disarmament policy, which he saw as a contributing factor in its defeat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEScott2012116\xe2\x80\x93118_343-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEScott2012116\xe2\x80\x93118-343">&#91;343&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-344" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-344">&#91;344&#93;</a></sup> The party formally voted to do so in October 1989.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChalmers199962_345-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChalmers199962-345">&#91;345&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Pro-independence Scottish political parties\xe2\x80\x94the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_National_Party" title="Scottish National Party">Scottish National Party</a> (SNP), <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Green_Party" title="Scottish Green Party">Scottish Green Party</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Socialist_Party" title="Scottish Socialist Party">Scottish Socialist Party</a> (SSP) and <a href="/wiki/Solidarity_(Scotland)" title="Solidarity (Scotland)">Solidarity</a>\xe2\x80\x94are opposed to the basing of the Trident system close to Glasgow, Scotland\'s largest city.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERitchie2016658\xe2\x80\x93659_346-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERitchie2016658\xe2\x80\x93659-346">&#91;346&#93;</a></sup> Some members and ex-members of those parties, such as <a href="/wiki/Tommy_Sheridan" title="Tommy Sheridan">Tommy Sheridan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lloyd_Quinan" title="Lloyd Quinan">Lloyd Quinan</a>, have taken part in blockades of the base.<sup id="cite_ref-347" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-347">&#91;347&#93;</a></sup> In the House of Commons vote in 2007, the majority of Scottish members of parliament (MPs) voted against upgrading the system, while a substantial majority of English, Welsh and Northern Irish MPs voted in favour.<sup id="cite_ref-348" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-348">&#91;348&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_posture">Nuclear posture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Nuclear posture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The UK has relaxed its nuclear posture since the <a href="/wiki/Collapse_of_the_Soviet_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Collapse of the Soviet Union">collapse of the Soviet Union</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a> government\'s 1998 <a href="/wiki/Strategic_Defence_Review" title="Strategic Defence Review">Strategic Defence Review</a> made reductions from the plans announced by the previous <a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a> government:\n</p>\n<ul><li>The stockpile of "operationally available warheads" was reduced from 300 to "less than 200"</li>\n<li>The final batch of missile bodies would not be purchased, limiting the fleet to 58.</li>\n<li>A submarine\'s load of warheads were reduced from 96 to 48. This reduced the explosive power of the warheads on a Vanguard class Trident submarine to "one third less than a Polaris submarine armed with Chevaline." However, 48 warheads per Trident submarine represents a 50% increase on the 32 warheads per submarine of Chevaline. Total explosive power has been in decline for decades as the accuracy of missiles has improved, therefore requiring less power to destroy each target. Trident can destroy 48 targets per submarine, as opposed to 32 targets that could be destroyed by Chevaline.</li>\n<li>Submarines\' missiles would not be targeted, but rather at several days "notice to fire".</li>\n<li>Although one submarine would always be on patrol it will operate on a "reduced day-to-day alert state". A major factor in maintaining a constant patrol is to avoid "misunderstanding or escalation if a Trident submarine were to sail during a period of crisis."<sup id="cite_ref-349" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-349">&#91;349&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>\n<p>Current UK posture is as it has been for many years; Trident SLBMs still provide the long-range strategic element. Until 1998 the aircraft-delivered, free-fall WE.177 bombs provided a sub-strategic option in addition to their designed function as tactical battlefield weapons. With the retirement of WE.177, a sub-strategic warhead is used with some (but not all) deployed Trident missiles. The 2010 Strategic Defence and Security Review further pledged to reduce its requirement for operationally available warheads from fewer than 160 to no more than 120.<sup id="cite_ref-350" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-350">&#91;350&#93;</a></sup> In a January 2015 written statement, Defence Secretary Michael Fallon reported that "all Vanguard Class SSBNs on continuous at-sea deterrent patrol now carry 40 nuclear warheads and no more than eight operational missiles".<sup id="cite_ref-351" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-351">&#91;351&#93;</a></sup> In April 2017 Defence Secretary <a href="/wiki/Michael_Fallon" title="Michael Fallon">Michael Fallon</a> confirmed that the UK would use nuclear weapons in a <a href="/wiki/Pre-emptive_nuclear_strike" title="Pre-emptive nuclear strike">pre-emptive nuclear strike</a> under "the most extreme circumstances".<sup id="cite_ref-independent-20170424_352-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-independent-20170424-352">&#91;352&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_weapons_control">Nuclear weapons control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Nuclear weapons control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WE.177_safety_keys.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/WE.177_safety_keys.jpg/220px-WE.177_safety_keys.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/WE.177_safety_keys.jpg/330px-WE.177_safety_keys.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/WE.177_safety_keys.jpg/440px-WE.177_safety_keys.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WE.177_safety_keys.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>WE.177 safety and arming keys</div></div></div>\n<p>The precise details of how a British Prime Minister would authorise a nuclear strike remain secret, although the principles of the Trident missile control system is believed to be based on the plan set up for Polaris in 1968, which has now been declassified. A closed-circuit television system was set up between 10 Downing Street and the SSBN Control Officer at the <a href="/wiki/Northwood_Headquarters" title="Northwood Headquarters">Northwood Headquarters</a> of the Royal Navy. Both the Prime Minister and the SSBN Control Officer would be able to see each other on their monitors when the command was given. If the link failed \xe2\x80\x93 for instance during a nuclear attack or when the PM was away from Downing Street \xe2\x80\x93 the Prime Minister would send an authentication code which could be verified at Northwood. The PM would then broadcast a firing order to the SSBN submarines via the Very Low Frequency radio station at Rugby. The UK has not deployed control equipment requiring codes to be sent before weapons can be used, such as the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Permissive_Action_Link" title="Permissive Action Link">Permissive Action Link</a>, which if installed would preclude the possibility that military officers could launch British nuclear weapons without authorisation.<sup id="cite_ref-MoD_statement_353-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MoD_statement-353">&#91;353&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-British_nuclear_weapon_control_354-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-British_nuclear_weapon_control-354">&#91;354&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC_press_release_355-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_press_release-355">&#91;355&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Until 1998, when it was withdrawn from service, the WE.177 bomb was armed with a standard <a href="/wiki/Tubular_pin_tumbler_lock" title="Tubular pin tumbler lock">tubular pin tumbler lock</a> (as used on bicycle locks) and a standard <a href="/wiki/Allen_key" class="mw-redirect" title="Allen key">allen key</a> was used to set yield and burst height. Currently, British Trident missile commanders are able to launch their missiles without authorisation, whereas their American counterparts cannot. At the end of the Cold War the U.S. Fail Safe Commission recommended installing devices to prevent rogue commanders persuading their crews to launch unauthorised nuclear attacks. This was endorsed by the Nuclear Posture Review and Trident missile Coded Control Devices were fitted to all U.S. SSBNs by 1997. These devices prevented an attack until a launch code had been sent by the Chiefs of Staff on behalf of the President. The UK took a decision not to install Trident CCDs or their equivalent on the grounds that an aggressor might be able to wipe out the British chain of command before a launch order had been sent.<sup id="cite_ref-MoD_statement_353-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MoD_statement-353">&#91;353&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-British_nuclear_weapon_control_354-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-British_nuclear_weapon_control-354">&#91;354&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC_press_release_355-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC_press_release-355">&#91;355&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DASO_Trident_missile_test_firing_on-board_HMS_Vigilant_MOD_45159461.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Photograph of a hand holding a pistol grip; the grip has a red trigger button and a coiled cable connected to its base." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/DASO_Trident_missile_test_firing_on-board_HMS_Vigilant_MOD_45159461.jpg/170px-DASO_Trident_missile_test_firing_on-board_HMS_Vigilant_MOD_45159461.jpg" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/DASO_Trident_missile_test_firing_on-board_HMS_Vigilant_MOD_45159461.jpg/255px-DASO_Trident_missile_test_firing_on-board_HMS_Vigilant_MOD_45159461.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/DASO_Trident_missile_test_firing_on-board_HMS_Vigilant_MOD_45159461.jpg/340px-DASO_Trident_missile_test_firing_on-board_HMS_Vigilant_MOD_45159461.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3280" data-file-height="4928" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DASO_Trident_missile_test_firing_on-board_HMS_Vigilant_MOD_45159461.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Weapons Engineer Officers Tactical Trigger used to launch a Trident Missile.  Taken in 2012 aboard <a href="/wiki/HMS_Vigilant_(S30)" title="HMS Vigilant (S30)">HMS <i>Vigilant</i></a> during a test launch of an unarmed Trident ballistic missile at sea.</div></div></div>\n<p>In December 2008 BBC Radio 4 made a programme titled <i>The Human Button</i>, providing new information on the manner in which the United Kingdom could launch its nuclear weapons, particularly relating to safeguards against a rogue launch.  The former <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_the_Defence_Staff_(United_Kingdom)" title="Chief of the Defence Staff (United Kingdom)">Chief of the Defence Staff</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_the_General_Staff_(United_Kingdom)" title="Chief of the General Staff (United Kingdom)">Chief of the General Staff</a>, General <a href="/wiki/Charles_Guthrie,_Baron_Guthrie_of_Craigiebank" title="Charles Guthrie, Baron Guthrie of Craigiebank">Lord Guthrie of Craigiebank</a>, explained that the highest level of safeguard was against a <a href="/wiki/Prime_minister" title="Prime minister">prime minister</a> ordering a launch without due cause. The constitutional structure of the United Kingdom provided some protection against such an occurrence, as while the Prime Minister is the chief executive and so practically commands the armed services, the formal commander-in-chief is the <a href="/wiki/British_monarchy" class="mw-redirect" title="British monarchy">monarch</a>, to whom the chief of the defence staff could appeal: "the chief of the defence staff, if he really did think the prime minister had gone mad, would make quite sure that that order was not obeyed... You have to remember that actually prime ministers give direction, they tell the chief of the defence staff what they want, but it\'s not prime ministers who actually tell a sailor to press a button in the middle of the Atlantic.  The armed forces are loyal, and we live in a democracy, but actually their ultimate authority is the <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_II" title="Elizabeth II">Queen</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-Whose_hand_is_on_the_button?_356-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whose_hand_is_on_the_button?-356">&#91;356&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The same interview pointed out that while the Prime Minister would have the constitutional authority to fire the Chief of the Defence Staff, he could not appoint a replacement as the position is appointed by the monarch.<sup id="cite_ref-Whose_hand_is_on_the_button?_356-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whose_hand_is_on_the_button?-356">&#91;356&#93;</a></sup> The programme also addressed the workings of the system; detailing that <a href="/wiki/Two-man_rule" title="Two-man rule">two persons are required</a> to authenticate each stage of the process before launching, with the submarine captain only able to access the firing trigger after two safes have been opened with keys held by the ship\'s executive and <a href="/wiki/Weapons_engineering" class="mw-redirect" title="Weapons engineering">weapons engineering</a> officers. It was explained that all Prime Ministers issue hand-written orders, termed the <a href="/wiki/Letters_of_last_resort" title="Letters of last resort">letters of last resort</a>, seen by their eyes only, sealed and stored within the safes of each of the four Royal Navy Vanguard class submarines. These notes instruct the submarine commander of what action to take in the event of the United Kingdom being attacked with nuclear weapons that destroy Her Majesty\'s Government in the United Kingdom and/or the chain of command.<sup id="cite_ref-357" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-357">&#91;357&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although the final orders of the Prime Minister are at his or her discretion, and no fixed options exist, there were four known options: retaliating with nuclear weapons, not retaliating with nuclear weapons, the submarine commander uses his own judgement, or the submarine commander places himself under <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> or <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australian</a> command if possible. This system of issuing notes containing orders in the event of the head of government\'s death is said to be unique to the United Kingdom (although the concept of written last orders, particularly of a ship\'s captain, is a naval tradition), with other nuclear powers using different procedures. The letters are destroyed unopened whenever a Prime Minister leaves office.<sup id="cite_ref-358" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-358">&#91;358&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>All former prime ministers have supported an "independent nuclear deterrent", including <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> and  <a href="/wiki/Theresa_May" title="Theresa May">Theresa May</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-359" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-359">&#91;359&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-360" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-360">&#91;360&#93;</a></sup> Only one, <a href="/wiki/Lord_Callaghan" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Callaghan">Lord Callaghan</a>, has given any insight on his orders; Callaghan stated that, although in a situation where nuclear weapon use was required \xe2\x80\x93 and thus the whole purpose and value of the weapon as a deterrent had failed \xe2\x80\x93 he would have ordered use of nuclear weapons, if needed: "...if we had got to that point, where it was, I felt it was necessary to do it, then I would have done it (used the weapon)...but if I had lived after pressing that button, I could have never forgiven myself."<sup id="cite_ref-BBCTOday_361-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCTOday-361">&#91;361&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Denis_Healey" title="Denis Healey">Lord Healey</a>, the Secretary of State for Defence and "alternate decision-taker" under Harold Wilson, said that in the event of Soviet nuclear weapons attacking the United Kingdom and the Prime Minister had been killed or incapacitated, he would not have ordered a retaliation.<sup id="cite_ref-BBCTOday_361-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBCTOday-361">&#91;361&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legality">Legality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Legality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/International_Court_of_Justice_advisory_opinion_on_the_Legality_of_the_Threat_or_Use_of_Nuclear_Weapons" title="International Court of Justice advisory opinion on the Legality of the Threat or Use of Nuclear Weapons">International Court of Justice advisory opinion on the Legality of the Threat or Use of Nuclear Weapons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Non-Proliferation_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear Non-Proliferation Treaty">Nuclear Non-Proliferation Treaty</a></div><p>\nThe United Kingdom is one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_states_with_nuclear_weapons#Five_nuclear-weapon_states_under_the_NPT" title="List of states with nuclear weapons">five nuclear-weapon states</a> legally recognised as such under the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_on_the_Non-Proliferation_of_Nuclear_Weapons" title="Treaty on the Non-Proliferation of Nuclear Weapons">Treaty on the Non-Proliferation of Nuclear Weapons</a> (NPT). As of  2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, nine countries have nuclear weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-362" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-362">&#91;362&#93;</a></sup> After the UK government announced its plans to refurbish its Trident missiles and build new submarines to carry them, it published a <a href="/wiki/White_paper" title="White paper">white paper</a> on <i>The Future of the United Kingdom\'s Nuclear Deterrent</i>, in which it stated that the renewal is fully compatible with the United Kingdom\'s treaty commitments and international law.<sup id="cite_ref-363" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-363">&#91;363&#93;</a></sup> At the start of the <a href="/wiki/British_House_of_Commons" class="mw-redirect" title="British House of Commons">House of Commons</a> debate to authorise the replacement of Trident, <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Beckett" title="Margaret Beckett">Margaret Beckett</a>, the Secretary of State for Foreign and Commonwealth Affairs, stated:</p><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Article VI of the NPT imposes an obligation on all states: "to pursue negotiations in good faith on effective measures relating to cessation of the nuclear arms race at an early date and to nuclear disarmament, and on a Treaty on general and complete disarmament". The NPT Review Conference held in 2000 agreed, by consensus, <a href="/wiki/13_steps" title="13 steps">13 practical steps</a> towards nuclear disarmament. The UK remains committed to these steps and is making progress on them. We have been disarming. Since the Cold War ended, we have withdrawn and dismantled our tactical maritime and airborne nuclear capabilities. We have terminated our nuclear capable Lance missiles and artillery. We have the smallest nuclear capability of any recognised nuclear weapon state accounting for less than one per cent of the global inventory. And we are the only nuclear weapon state that relies on a single nuclear system.<sup id="cite_ref-364" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-364">&#91;364&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>The subsequent vote was won overwhelmingly, including unanimous support from the opposition Conservative Party.<sup id="cite_ref-Blair_267-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blair-267">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup>  The Government\'s position remained that it was abiding by the NPT in renewing Trident, and Britain has the right to possess nuclear weapons, a position reiterated by Tony Blair on 21 February 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-365" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-365">&#91;365&#93;</a></sup> Only the United Kingdom has expressed its opposition to the establishment of a new legally binding treaty to prevent the threat or use of nuclear weapons against non-nuclear states,<sup id="cite_ref-UN_ARES5377_page42_366-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UN_ARES5377_page42-366">&#91;366&#93;</a></sup> through its vote in the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">United Nations General Assembly</a> in 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-367" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-367">&#91;367&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nuclear_weapons_and_the_United_Kingdom&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEGowing1964236\xe2\x80\x93239-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGowing1964236\xe2\x80\x93239_33-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a
